Thus, 13 divides \( 5^{13} + 8^{13} \). 2. **Using LTE for Higher Powers**: - For \( n = 13^k \), we apply LTE. Let \( a = 5 \), \( b = 8 \), and \( p = 13 \). - Since \( 13 \mid 5 + 8 \) and 13 does not divide 5 or 8, LTE conditions are satisfied. - LTE states: \( v_{13}(5^{13^k} + 8^{13^k}) = v_{13}(5 + 8) + v_{13}(13^k) \). - Here, \( v_{13}(5 + 8) = 1 \) and \( v_{13}(13^k) = k \). - Therefore, \( v_{13}(5^{13^k} + 8^{13^k}) = 1 + k \), meaning \( 13^{k+1} \mid 5^{13^k} + 8^{13^k} \). 3. **Conclusion**: - Since \( 13^{k+1} \mid 5^{13^k} + 8^{13^k} \), it follows that \( 13^k \mid 5^{13^k} + 8^{13^k} \). - This holds for all \( k \geq 1 \), confirming that if \( n \) is a power of 13, then \( n \mid 5^n + 8^n \). Thus, the statement is true, and the final answer is \(\boxed{True}\).

Let \( a = 5 \), \( b = 8 \), and \( p = 13 \). - Since \( 13 \mid 5 + 8 \) and 13 does not divide 5 or 8, LTE conditions are satisfied. - LTE states: \( v_{13}(5^{13^k} + 8^{13^k}) = v_{13}(5 + 8) + v_{13}(13^k) \). - Here, \( v_{13}(5 + 8) = 1 \) and \( v_{13}(13^k) = k \). - Therefore, \( v_{13}(5^{13^k} + 8^{13^k}) = 1 + k \), meaning \( 13^{k+1} \mid 5^{13^k} + 8^{13^k} \). 3. **Conclusion**: - Since \( 13^{k+1} \mid 5^{13^k} + 8^{13^k} \), it follows that \( 13^k \mid 5^{13^k} + 8^{13^k} \). - This holds for all \( k \geq 1 \), confirming that if \( n \) is a power of 13, then \( n \mid 5^n + 8^n \). Thus, the statement is true, and the final answer is \(\boxed{True}\).

Thus, 13 divides \( 5^{13} + 8^{13} \). 2. **Using LTE for Higher Powers**: - For \( n = 13^k \), we apply LTE. Thus, the statement is true, and the final answer is \(\boxed{True}\).

Thus, 13 divides \( 5^{13} + 8^{13} \). 2. **Using LTE for Higher Powers**: - For \( n = 13^k \), we apply LTE. Let \( a = 5 \), \( b = 8 \), and \( p = 13 \). - Since \( 13 \mid 5 + 8 \) and 13 does not divide 5 or 8, LTE conditions are satisfied. - LTE states: \( v_{13}(5^{13^k} + 8^{13^k}) = v_{13}(5 + 8) + v_{13}(13^k) \). - Here, \( v_{13}(5 + 8) = 1 \) and \( v_{13}(13^k) = k \). - Therefore, \( v_{13}(5^{13^k} + 8^{13^k}) = 1 + k \), meaning \( 13^{k+1} \mid 5^{13^k} + 8^{13^k} \). 3. **Conclusion**: - Since \( 13^{k+1} \mid 5^{13^k} + 8^{13^k} \), it follows that \( 13^k \mid 5^{13^k} + 8^{13^k} \). - This holds for all \( k \geq 1 \), confirming that if \( n \) is a power of 13, then \( n \mid 5^n + 8^n \). Thus, the statement is true, and the final answer is \(\boxed{True}\).

Thus, 13 divides \( 5^{13} + 8^{13} \). 2. **Using LTE for Higher Powers**: - For \( n = 13^k \), we apply LTE. Thus, the statement is true, and the final answer is \(\boxed{True}\).

Thus, 13 divides \( 5^{13} + 8^{13} \). 2. **Using LTE for Higher Powers**: - For \( n = 13^k \), we apply LTE. Thus, the statement is true, and the final answer is \(\boxed{True}\).

Problem: Evaluate the contour integral \( \int_C \frac{z}{2z^2+1} \, dz \) where \( C \) is the unit circle centered at the origin. Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: \boxed{0}

Reference Answer: Okay, so I need to evaluate the contour integral of the function \( \frac{z}{2z^2 + 1} \) over the unit circle centered at the origin. Hmm, let's start by recalling some complex analysis. Contour integrals often involve residues if the function is meromorphic inside the contour. The unit circle is a closed contour, so I should check if the integrand has any poles inside the unit circle and then apply the residue theorem. First, let's analyze the denominator \( 2z^2 + 1 \). To find the poles, set the denominator equal to zero: \( 2z^2 + 1 = 0 \). Solving for z gives \( z^2 = -1/2 \), so \( z = \pm \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \). Wait, let me check that again. If \( 2z^2 = -1 \), then \( z^2 = -1/2 \), so the square roots are \( z = \pm \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \). Right, because \( (\pm \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}})^2 = -1/2 \). Now, I need to determine if these poles are inside the unit circle. The magnitude of each pole is \( | \pm \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} | = \frac{1}{\sqrt{2}} \approx 0.707 \), which is less than 1. So both poles are indeed inside the unit circle. Therefore, the integrand has two simple poles inside the contour C, and by the residue theorem, the integral will be \( 2\pi i \) times the sum of the residues at these poles. But before I jump into calculating residues, maybe I can simplify the integrand. Let me write the integrand as \( \frac{z}{2z^2 + 1} \). Let's factor the denominator: \( 2z^2 + 1 = 2(z^2 + \frac{1}{2}) = 2(z - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}})(z + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}) \). So the integrand becomes \( \frac{z}{2(z - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}})(z + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}})} \). That might help in calculating residues. Alternatively, maybe partial fractions? Let me see. If I want to decompose \( \frac{z}{2z^2 + 1} \) into partial fractions. Let’s set \( \frac{z}{2z^2 + 1} = \frac{A}{z - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}} + \frac{B}{z + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}} \). Multiply both sides by \( 2z^2 + 1 \), which is \( 2(z - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}})(z + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}) \), gives: \( z = A(z + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}) + B(z - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}) \). To find A and B, we can substitute z values that simplify the equation. Let me plug in \( z = \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \): Left side: \( \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \). Right side: \( A( \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} ) + B(0) = A( \frac{2i}{\sqrt{2}} ) = A(i \sqrt{2}) \). Therefore, \( A(i \sqrt{2}) = \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \Rightarrow A = \frac{i/\sqrt{2}}{i \sqrt{2}} = \frac{1}{2} \). Similarly, plug in \( z = -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \): Left side: \( -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \). Right side: \( A(0) + B(-\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} ) = B( -\frac{2i}{\sqrt{2}} ) = B(-i \sqrt{2}) \). Therefore, \( -i \sqrt{2} B = -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \Rightarrow B = \frac{ -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} }{ -i \sqrt{2} } = \frac{1}{2} \). So the partial fractions decomposition is \( \frac{1}{2} \left( \frac{1}{z - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}} + \frac{1}{z + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}} \right) \). Therefore, the integral becomes \( \frac{1}{2} \int_C \left( \frac{1}{z - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}} + \frac{1}{z + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}} \right) dz \). Now, integrating each term separately. The integral of \( \frac{1}{z - a} \) around a contour enclosing a is \( 2\pi i \), by Cauchy's integral formula. Since both \( \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \) and \( -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \) are inside the unit circle, each integral will contribute \( 2\pi i \). Therefore, the total integral is \( \frac{1}{2} (2\pi i + 2\pi i) = \frac{1}{2}(4\pi i) = 2\pi i \). So, is the answer \( 2\pi i \)? Wait, but let me verify this with residue theorem instead of partial fractions. Let's recall that for a function \( f(z) = \frac{z}{2z^2 + 1} \), the residues at the poles \( z_0 \) are given by \( \text{Res}(f, z_0) = \lim_{z \to z_0} (z - z_0) f(z) \). First, at \( z = \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \): \( \text{Res}(f, \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}) = \lim_{z \to \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}} (z - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}) \cdot \frac{z}{2z^2 + 1} \). But since \( 2z^2 + 1 = 2(z - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}})(z + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}) \), we can write: \( \text{Res}(f, \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}) = \lim_{z \to \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}} \frac{z}{2(z + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}})} \). Substituting \( z = \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \): \( \frac{ \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} }{ 2( \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} ) } = \frac{ \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} }{ 2( \frac{2i}{\sqrt{2}} ) } = \frac{ \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} }{ \frac{4i}{\sqrt{2}} } = \frac{1}{4} \). Similarly, at \( z = -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \): \( \text{Res}(f, -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}) = \lim_{z \to -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}} (z + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}) \cdot \frac{z}{2z^2 + 1} \). Which becomes: \( \lim_{z \to -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}} \frac{z}{2(z - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}})} \). Substituting \( z = -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \): \( \frac{ -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} }{ 2( -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} ) } = \frac{ -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} }{ 2( -\frac{2i}{\sqrt{2}} ) } = \frac{ -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} }{ -\frac{4i}{\sqrt{2}} } = \frac{1}{4} \). Therefore, each residue is \( \frac{1}{4} \), and the sum is \( \frac{1}{4} + \frac{1}{4} = \frac{1}{2} \). Then, the integral is \( 2\pi i \times \frac{1}{2} = \pi i \). Wait, but earlier with partial fractions, I got \( 2\pi i \). Which one is correct? There's a discrepancy here. Hmm, that's a problem. Let me check my calculations again. First, in the partial fractions approach: I had the integrand as \( \frac{1}{2} \left( \frac{1}{z - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}} + \frac{1}{z + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}} \right) \). Then, integrating each term over the unit circle. The integral of \( \frac{1}{z - a} \) around a circle enclosing a is indeed \( 2\pi i \), so integrating \( \frac{1}{z - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}} \) gives \( 2\pi i \), same with the other term. Adding them gives \( 4\pi i \), multiplied by \( 1/2 \) gives \( 2\pi i \). But in the residue approach, each residue is \( 1/4 \), so sum is \( 1/2 \), leading to \( 2\pi i \times 1/2 = \pi i \). Hmm, conflicting answers. So I must have made a mistake in one of the methods. Wait, let me check the residue calculation again. Starting with the residue at \( z = \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \): Original function: \( \frac{z}{2z^2 + 1} \). Denominator factors as \( 2(z - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}})(z + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}) \). Therefore, near \( z = \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \), the function can be written as \( \frac{z}{2(z - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}})(z + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}})} \). So, the residue is the coefficient of \( \frac{1}{z - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}} \), which is \( \frac{z}{2(z + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}})} \) evaluated at \( z = \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \). So plugging in, that's \( \frac{ \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} }{ 2( \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} ) } = \frac{ \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} }{ 2( \frac{2i}{\sqrt{2}} ) } = \frac{ \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} }{ \frac{4i}{\sqrt{2}} } \). The numerator is \( i/\sqrt{2} \), denominator is \( 4i/\sqrt{2} \). So dividing these, the \( i \) cancels, \( \sqrt{2} \) cancels, leaving \( 1/4 \). So that seems correct. Similarly, at \( z = -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \), the residue is \( \frac{ -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} }{ 2( -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} ) } = \frac{ -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} }{ 2( -\frac{2i}{\sqrt{2}} ) } = \frac{ -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} }{ -\frac{4i}{\sqrt{2}} } = 1/4 \). So, in conclusion, the integral \( \int_C \frac{z}{2z^2 +1} \, dz \) equals \( \pi i \). **Final Answer** The value of the contour integral is \boxed{\pi i}. </think> To evaluate the contour integral \( \int_C \frac{z}{2z^2 + 1} \, dz \) where \( C \) is the unit circle centered at the origin, we start by identifying the poles of the integrand. The denominator \( 2z^2 + 1 = 0 \) gives solutions \( z = \pm \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \). Both poles have a magnitude of \( \frac{1}{\sqrt{2}} \), which is less than 1, so they lie inside the unit circle. Using the residue theorem, we calculate the residues at these poles. The integrand can be written as \( \frac{z}{2(z - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}})(z + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}})} \). For a simple pole \( z_0 \), the residue is given by: \[ \text{Res}(f, z_0) = \lim_{z \to z_0} (z - z_0) \cdot \frac{z}{2(z - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}})(z + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}})} \] For \( z = \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \): \[ \text{Res}\left(\frac{z}{2z^2 + 1}, \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}\right) = \frac{\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}}{2 \left( \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \right)} = \frac{\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}}{2 \cdot \frac{2i}{\sqrt{2}}} = \frac{1}{4} \] For \( z = -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \): \[ \text{Res}\left(\frac{z}{2z^2 + 1}, -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}\right) = \frac{-\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}}{2 \left( -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \right)} = \frac{-\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}}{2 \cdot -\frac{2i}{\sqrt{2}}} = \frac{1}{4} \] Summing these residues gives \( \frac{1}{4} + \frac{1}{4} = \frac{1}{2} \). By the residue theorem, the integral is \( 2\pi i \) times the sum of the residues: \[ \int_C \frac{z}{2z^2 + 1} \, dz = 2\pi i \cdot \frac{1}{2} = \pi i \] Thus, the value of the contour integral is \(\boxed{\pi i}\).

Combine like terms: (2A + 2B) z + (2A - 2B) \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}. Set equal to left side: z + 0. Therefore, equations: Coefficient of z: 2A + 2B = 1. Coefficient of constant term: (2A - 2B) \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} = 0. From the constant term equation: 2A - 2B = 0 ⇒ A = B. From the z coefficient: 2A + 2A = 1 ⇒ 4A = 1 ⇒ A = 1/4, B = 1/4. Therefore, the partial fractions decomposition is \( \frac{1/4}{z - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}} + \frac{1/4}{z + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}} \). Wait, so earlier I missed the factor of 2 in the denominator. The original denominator had a factor of 2, so when decomposing, the equation should be multiplied by the entire denominator, which includes the 2, hence leading to coefficients A and B being 1/4 each instead of 1/2. So my initial partial fractions decomposition was incorrect because I forgot the 2 in the denominator. Therefore, integrating gives \( \frac{1}{4} \times 2\pi i + \frac{1}{4} \times 2\pi i = \frac{\pi i}{2} + \frac{\pi i}{2} = \pi i \), which matches the residue theorem and Laurent series results. So my mistake was forgetting the 2 when setting up the partial fractions. That was the error. So the correct answer is \( \pi i \). Therefore, the integral evaluates to \( \pi i \). But let me confirm once more. Using residues, sum of residues is 1/4 + 1/4 = 1/2, multiplied by \( 2\pi i \) gives \( \pi i \). Laurent series gave the same. And corrected partial fractions also lead to \( \pi i \). So that's consistent. Therefore, the correct value is \( \pi i \). So, in conclusion, the integral \( \int_C \frac{z}{2z^2 +1} \, dz \) equals \( \pi i \). **Final Answer** The value of the contour integral is \boxed{\pi i}. </think> To evaluate the contour integral \( \int_C \frac{z}{2z^2 + 1} \, dz \) where \( C \) is the unit circle centered at the origin, we start by identifying the poles of the integrand. The denominator \( 2z^2 + 1 = 0 \) gives solutions \( z = \pm \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \). Both poles have a magnitude of \( \frac{1}{\sqrt{2}} \), which is less than 1, so they lie inside the unit circle. Using the residue theorem, we calculate the residues at these poles. The integrand can be written as \( \frac{z}{2(z - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}})(z + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}})} \). For a simple pole \( z_0 \), the residue is given by: \[ \text{Res}(f, z_0) = \lim_{z \to z_0} (z - z_0) \cdot \frac{z}{2(z - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}})(z + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}})} \] For \( z = \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \): \[ \text{Res}\left(\frac{z}{2z^2 + 1}, \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}\right) = \frac{\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}}{2 \left( \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \right)} = \frac{\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}}{2 \cdot \frac{2i}{\sqrt{2}}} = \frac{1}{4} \] For \( z = -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \): \[ \text{Res}\left(\frac{z}{2z^2 + 1}, -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}\right) = \frac{-\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}}{2 \left( -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \right)} = \frac{-\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}}{2 \cdot -\frac{2i}{\sqrt{2}}} = \frac{1}{4} \] Summing these residues gives \( \frac{1}{4} + \frac{1}{4} = \frac{1}{2} \). By the residue theorem, the integral is \( 2\pi i \) times the sum of the residues: \[ \int_C \frac{z}{2z^2 + 1} \, dz = 2\pi i \cdot \frac{1}{2} = \pi i \] Thus, the value of the contour integral is \(\boxed{\pi i}\).

Right side: \( A( \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} ) + B(0) = A( \frac{2i}{\sqrt{2}} ) = A(i \sqrt{2}) \). Therefore, \( A(i \sqrt{2}) = \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \Rightarrow A = \frac{i/\sqrt{2}}{i \sqrt{2}} = \frac{1}{2} \). Similarly, plug in \( z = -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \): Left side: \( -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \). Right side: \( A(0) + B(-\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} ) = B( -\frac{2i}{\sqrt{2}} ) = B(-i \sqrt{2}) \). Since both \( \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \) and \( -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \) are inside the unit circle, each integral will contribute \( 2\pi i \). Therefore, the total integral is \( \frac{1}{2} (2\pi i + 2\pi i) = \frac{1}{2}(4\pi i) = 2\pi i \). So, is the answer \( 2\pi i \)? Wait, but let me verify this with residue theorem instead of partial fractions. Let's recall that for a function \( f(z) = \frac{z}{2z^2 + 1} \), the residues at the poles \( z_0 \) are given by \( \text{Res}(f, z_0) = \lim_{z \to z_0} (z - z_0) f(z) \). First, at \( z = \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \): \( \text{Res}(f, \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}) = \lim_{z \to \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}} (z - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}) \cdot \frac{z}{2z^2 + 1} \). But since \( 2z^2 + 1 = 2(z - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}})(z + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}) \), we can write: \( \text{Res}(f, \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}) = \lim_{z \to \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}} \frac{z}{2(z + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}})} \). Therefore, each residue is \( \frac{1}{4} \), and the sum is \( \frac{1}{4} + \frac{1}{4} = \frac{1}{2} \). Then, the integral is \( 2\pi i \times \frac{1}{2} = \pi i \). Wait, but earlier with partial fractions, I got \( 2\pi i \). Which one is correct? There's a discrepancy here. Hmm, that's a problem. Let me check my calculations again. The denominator \( 2z^2 + 1 = 0 \) gives solutions \( z = \pm \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \). Both poles have a magnitude of \( \frac{1}{\sqrt{2}} \), which is less than 1, so they lie inside the unit circle. Using the residue theorem, we calculate the residues at these poles. The integrand can be written as \( \frac{z}{2(z - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}})(z + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}})} \). For a simple pole \( z_0 \), the residue is given by: \[ \text{Res}(f, z_0) = \lim_{z \to z_0} (z - z_0) \cdot \frac{z}{2(z - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}})(z + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}})} \] For \( z = \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \): \[ \text{Res}\left(\frac{z}{2z^2 + 1}, \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}\right) = \frac{\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}}{2 \left( \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \right)} = \frac{\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}}{2 \cdot \frac{2i}{\sqrt{2}}} = \frac{1}{4} \] For \( z = -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \): \[ \text{Res}\left(\frac{z}{2z^2 + 1}, -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}\right) = \frac{-\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}}{2 \left( -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \right)} = \frac{-\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}}{2 \cdot -\frac{2i}{\sqrt{2}}} = \frac{1}{4} \] Summing these residues gives \( \frac{1}{4} + \frac{1}{4} = \frac{1}{2} \). By the residue theorem, the integral is \( 2\pi i \) times the sum of the residues: \[ \int_C \frac{z}{2z^2 + 1} \, dz = 2\pi i \cdot \frac{1}{2} = \pi i \] Thus, the value of the contour integral is \(\boxed{\pi i}\).

So, in conclusion, the integral \( \int_C \frac{z}{2z^2 +1} \, dz \) equals \( \pi i \). **Final Answer** The value of the contour integral is \boxed{\pi i}. </think> To evaluate the contour integral \( \int_C \frac{z}{2z^2 + 1} \, dz \) where \( C \) is the unit circle centered at the origin, we start by identifying the poles of the integrand. The denominator \( 2z^2 + 1 = 0 \) gives solutions \( z = \pm \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \). Both poles have a magnitude of \( \frac{1}{\sqrt{2}} \), which is less than 1, so they lie inside the unit circle. Using the residue theorem, we calculate the residues at these poles. The integrand can be written as \( \frac{z}{2(z - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}})(z + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}})} \). For a simple pole \( z_0 \), the residue is given by: \[ \text{Res}(f, z_0) = \lim_{z \to z_0} (z - z_0) \cdot \frac{z}{2(z - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}})(z + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}})} \] For \( z = \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \): \[ \text{Res}\left(\frac{z}{2z^2 + 1}, \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}\right) = \frac{\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}}{2 \left( \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \right)} = \frac{\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}}{2 \cdot \frac{2i}{\sqrt{2}}} = \frac{1}{4} \] For \( z = -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \): \[ \text{Res}\left(\frac{z}{2z^2 + 1}, -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}\right) = \frac{-\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}}{2 \left( -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \right)} = \frac{-\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}}{2 \cdot -\frac{2i}{\sqrt{2}}} = \frac{1}{4} \] Summing these residues gives \( \frac{1}{4} + \frac{1}{4} = \frac{1}{2} \). By the residue theorem, the integral is \( 2\pi i \) times the sum of the residues: \[ \int_C \frac{z}{2z^2 + 1} \, dz = 2\pi i \cdot \frac{1}{2} = \pi i \] Thus, the value of the contour integral is \(\boxed{\pi i}\).

Multiply both sides by \( 2(z - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}})(z + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}) \): Left side: z. Right side: 2A(z + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}) + 2B(z - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}). Therefore, equation: z = 2A(z + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}) + 2B(z - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}). Expand right side: 2A z + 2A \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} + 2B z - 2B \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}. Combine like terms: (2A + 2B) z + (2A - 2B) \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}. Set equal to left side: z + 0. Therefore, equations: Coefficient of z: 2A + 2B = 1. Coefficient of constant term: (2A - 2B) \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} = 0. From the constant term equation: 2A - 2B = 0 ⇒ A = B. From the z coefficient: 2A + 2A = 1 ⇒ 4A = 1 ⇒ A = 1/4, B = 1/4. Therefore, the partial fractions decomposition is \( \frac{1/4}{z - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}} + \frac{1/4}{z + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}} \). Wait, so earlier I missed the factor of 2 in the denominator. The original denominator had a factor of 2, so when decomposing, the equation should be multiplied by the entire denominator, which includes the 2, hence leading to coefficients A and B being 1/4 each instead of 1/2. So my initial partial fractions decomposition was incorrect because I forgot the 2 in the denominator. Therefore, integrating gives \( \frac{1}{4} \times 2\pi i + \frac{1}{4} \times 2\pi i = \frac{\pi i}{2} + \frac{\pi i}{2} = \pi i \), which matches the residue theorem and Laurent series results. So my mistake was forgetting the 2 when setting up the partial fractions. That was the error. So the correct answer is \( \pi i \). Therefore, the integral evaluates to \( \pi i \). But let me confirm once more. Using residues, sum of residues is 1/4 + 1/4 = 1/2, multiplied by \( 2\pi i \) gives \( \pi i \). Laurent series gave the same. And corrected partial fractions also lead to \( \pi i \). So that's consistent. Therefore, the correct value is \( \pi i \). So, in conclusion, the integral \( \int_C \frac{z}{2z^2 +1} \, dz \) equals \( \pi i \). **Final Answer** The value of the contour integral is \boxed{\pi i}. </think> To evaluate the contour integral \( \int_C \frac{z}{2z^2 + 1} \, dz \) where \( C \) is the unit circle centered at the origin, we start by identifying the poles of the integrand. The denominator \( 2z^2 + 1 = 0 \) gives solutions \( z = \pm \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \). Both poles have a magnitude of \( \frac{1}{\sqrt{2}} \), which is less than 1, so they lie inside the unit circle. Using the residue theorem, we calculate the residues at these poles. The integrand can be written as \( \frac{z}{2(z - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}})(z + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}})} \). For a simple pole \( z_0 \), the residue is given by: \[ \text{Res}(f, z_0) = \lim_{z \to z_0} (z - z_0) \cdot \frac{z}{2(z - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}})(z + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}})} \] For \( z = \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \): \[ \text{Res}\left(\frac{z}{2z^2 + 1}, \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}\right) = \frac{\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}}{2 \left( \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \right)} = \frac{\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}}{2 \cdot \frac{2i}{\sqrt{2}}} = \frac{1}{4} \] For \( z = -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \): \[ \text{Res}\left(\frac{z}{2z^2 + 1}, -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}\right) = \frac{-\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}}{2 \left( -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \right)} = \frac{-\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}}{2 \cdot -\frac{2i}{\sqrt{2}}} = \frac{1}{4} \] Summing these residues gives \( \frac{1}{4} + \frac{1}{4} = \frac{1}{2} \). By the residue theorem, the integral is \( 2\pi i \) times the sum of the residues: \[ \int_C \frac{z}{2z^2 + 1} \, dz = 2\pi i \cdot \frac{1}{2} = \pi i \] Thus, the value of the contour integral is \(\boxed{\pi i}\).

The denominator \( 2z^2 + 1 = 0 \) gives solutions \( z = \pm \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \). Both poles have a magnitude of \( \frac{1}{\sqrt{2}} \), which is less than 1, so they lie inside the unit circle. Using the residue theorem, we calculate the residues at these poles. The integrand can be written as \( \frac{z}{2(z - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}})(z + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}})} \). For a simple pole \( z_0 \), the residue is given by: \[ \text{Res}(f, z_0) = \lim_{z \to z_0} (z - z_0) \cdot \frac{z}{2(z - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}})(z + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}})} \] For \( z = \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \): \[ \text{Res}\left(\frac{z}{2z^2 + 1}, \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}\right) = \frac{\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}}{2 \left( \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \right)} = \frac{\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}}{2 \cdot \frac{2i}{\sqrt{2}}} = \frac{1}{4} \] For \( z = -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \): \[ \text{Res}\left(\frac{z}{2z^2 + 1}, -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}\right) = \frac{-\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}}{2 \left( -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \right)} = \frac{-\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}}{2 \cdot -\frac{2i}{\sqrt{2}}} = \frac{1}{4} \] Summing these residues gives \( \frac{1}{4} + \frac{1}{4} = \frac{1}{2} \). By the residue theorem, the integral is \( 2\pi i \) times the sum of the residues: \[ \int_C \frac{z}{2z^2 + 1} \, dz = 2\pi i \cdot \frac{1}{2} = \pi i \] Thus, the value of the contour integral is \(\boxed{\pi i}\).

The numerator is \( i/\sqrt{2} \), denominator is \( 4i/\sqrt{2} \). So dividing these, the \( i \) cancels, \( \sqrt{2} \) cancels, leaving \( 1/4 \). So that seems correct. Similarly, at \( z = -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \), the residue is \( \frac{ -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} }{ 2( -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} ) } = \frac{ -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} }{ 2( -\frac{2i}{\sqrt{2}} ) } = \frac{ -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} }{ -\frac{4i}{\sqrt{2}} } = 1/4 \). So, in conclusion, the integral \( \int_C \frac{z}{2z^2 +1} \, dz \) equals \( \pi i \). **Final Answer** The value of the contour integral is \boxed{\pi i}. </think> To evaluate the contour integral \( \int_C \frac{z}{2z^2 + 1} \, dz \) where \( C \) is the unit circle centered at the origin, we start by identifying the poles of the integrand. The denominator \( 2z^2 + 1 = 0 \) gives solutions \( z = \pm \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \). Both poles have a magnitude of \( \frac{1}{\sqrt{2}} \), which is less than 1, so they lie inside the unit circle. Using the residue theorem, we calculate the residues at these poles. The integrand can be written as \( \frac{z}{2(z - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}})(z + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}})} \). For a simple pole \( z_0 \), the residue is given by: \[ \text{Res}(f, z_0) = \lim_{z \to z_0} (z - z_0) \cdot \frac{z}{2(z - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}})(z + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}})} \] For \( z = \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \): \[ \text{Res}\left(\frac{z}{2z^2 + 1}, \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}\right) = \frac{\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}}{2 \left( \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \right)} = \frac{\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}}{2 \cdot \frac{2i}{\sqrt{2}}} = \frac{1}{4} \] For \( z = -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \): \[ \text{Res}\left(\frac{z}{2z^2 + 1}, -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}\right) = \frac{-\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}}{2 \left( -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \right)} = \frac{-\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}}{2 \cdot -\frac{2i}{\sqrt{2}}} = \frac{1}{4} \] Summing these residues gives \( \frac{1}{4} + \frac{1}{4} = \frac{1}{2} \). By the residue theorem, the integral is \( 2\pi i \) times the sum of the residues: \[ \int_C \frac{z}{2z^2 + 1} \, dz = 2\pi i \cdot \frac{1}{2} = \pi i \] Thus, the value of the contour integral is \(\boxed{\pi i}\).

The denominator \( 2z^2 + 1 = 0 \) gives solutions \( z = \pm \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \). Both poles have a magnitude of \( \frac{1}{\sqrt{2}} \), which is less than 1, so they lie inside the unit circle. Using the residue theorem, we calculate the residues at these poles. The integrand can be written as \( \frac{z}{2(z - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}})(z + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}})} \). For a simple pole \( z_0 \), the residue is given by: \[ \text{Res}(f, z_0) = \lim_{z \to z_0} (z - z_0) \cdot \frac{z}{2(z - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}})(z + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}})} \] For \( z = \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \): \[ \text{Res}\left(\frac{z}{2z^2 + 1}, \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}\right) = \frac{\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}}{2 \left( \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \right)} = \frac{\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}}{2 \cdot \frac{2i}{\sqrt{2}}} = \frac{1}{4} \] For \( z = -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \): \[ \text{Res}\left(\frac{z}{2z^2 + 1}, -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}\right) = \frac{-\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}}{2 \left( -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \right)} = \frac{-\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}}{2 \cdot -\frac{2i}{\sqrt{2}}} = \frac{1}{4} \] Summing these residues gives \( \frac{1}{4} + \frac{1}{4} = \frac{1}{2} \). By the residue theorem, the integral is \( 2\pi i \) times the sum of the residues: \[ \int_C \frac{z}{2z^2 + 1} \, dz = 2\pi i \cdot \frac{1}{2} = \pi i \] Thus, the value of the contour integral is \(\boxed{\pi i}\).

So the integrand becomes \( \frac{z}{2(z - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}})(z + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}})} \). That might help in calculating residues. Alternatively, maybe partial fractions? Let me see. If I want to decompose \( \frac{z}{2z^2 + 1} \) into partial fractions. Let’s set \( \frac{z}{2z^2 + 1} = \frac{A}{z - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}} + \frac{B}{z + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}} \). Multiply both sides by \( 2z^2 + 1 \), which is \( 2(z - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}})(z + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}) \), gives: \( z = A(z + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}) + B(z - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}) \). To find A and B, we can substitute z values that simplify the equation. Let me plug in \( z = \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \): Left side: \( \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \). First, at \( z = \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \): \( \text{Res}(f, \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}) = \lim_{z \to \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}} (z - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}) \cdot \frac{z}{2z^2 + 1} \). Substituting \( z = \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \): \( \frac{ \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} }{ 2( \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} ) } = \frac{ \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} }{ 2( \frac{2i}{\sqrt{2}} ) } = \frac{ \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} }{ \frac{4i}{\sqrt{2}} } = \frac{1}{4} \). The denominator \( 2z^2 + 1 = 0 \) gives solutions \( z = \pm \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \). Both poles have a magnitude of \( \frac{1}{\sqrt{2}} \), which is less than 1, so they lie inside the unit circle. Using the residue theorem, we calculate the residues at these poles. The integrand can be written as \( \frac{z}{2(z - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}})(z + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}})} \). For a simple pole \( z_0 \), the residue is given by: \[ \text{Res}(f, z_0) = \lim_{z \to z_0} (z - z_0) \cdot \frac{z}{2(z - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}})(z + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}})} \] For \( z = \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \): \[ \text{Res}\left(\frac{z}{2z^2 + 1}, \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}\right) = \frac{\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}}{2 \left( \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \right)} = \frac{\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}}{2 \cdot \frac{2i}{\sqrt{2}}} = \frac{1}{4} \] For \( z = -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \): \[ \text{Res}\left(\frac{z}{2z^2 + 1}, -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}\right) = \frac{-\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}}{2 \left( -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \right)} = \frac{-\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}}{2 \cdot -\frac{2i}{\sqrt{2}}} = \frac{1}{4} \] Summing these residues gives \( \frac{1}{4} + \frac{1}{4} = \frac{1}{2} \). By the residue theorem, the integral is \( 2\pi i \) times the sum of the residues: \[ \int_C \frac{z}{2z^2 + 1} \, dz = 2\pi i \cdot \frac{1}{2} = \pi i \] Thus, the value of the contour integral is \(\boxed{\pi i}\).

So plugging in, that's \( \frac{ \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} }{ 2( \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} ) } = \frac{ \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} }{ 2( \frac{2i}{\sqrt{2}} ) } = \frac{ \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} }{ \frac{4i}{\sqrt{2}} } \). The numerator is \( i/\sqrt{2} \), denominator is \( 4i/\sqrt{2} \). So dividing these, the \( i \) cancels, \( \sqrt{2} \) cancels, leaving \( 1/4 \). So that seems correct. Similarly, at \( z = -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \), the residue is \( \frac{ -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} }{ 2( -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} ) } = \frac{ -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} }{ 2( -\frac{2i}{\sqrt{2}} ) } = \frac{ -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} }{ -\frac{4i}{\sqrt{2}} } = 1/4 \). So, in conclusion, the integral \( \int_C \frac{z}{2z^2 +1} \, dz \) equals \( \pi i \). **Final Answer** The value of the contour integral is \boxed{\pi i}. </think> To evaluate the contour integral \( \int_C \frac{z}{2z^2 + 1} \, dz \) where \( C \) is the unit circle centered at the origin, we start by identifying the poles of the integrand. The denominator \( 2z^2 + 1 = 0 \) gives solutions \( z = \pm \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \). Both poles have a magnitude of \( \frac{1}{\sqrt{2}} \), which is less than 1, so they lie inside the unit circle. Using the residue theorem, we calculate the residues at these poles. The integrand can be written as \( \frac{z}{2(z - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}})(z + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}})} \). For a simple pole \( z_0 \), the residue is given by: \[ \text{Res}(f, z_0) = \lim_{z \to z_0} (z - z_0) \cdot \frac{z}{2(z - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}})(z + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}})} \] For \( z = \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \): \[ \text{Res}\left(\frac{z}{2z^2 + 1}, \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}\right) = \frac{\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}}{2 \left( \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \right)} = \frac{\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}}{2 \cdot \frac{2i}{\sqrt{2}}} = \frac{1}{4} \] For \( z = -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \): \[ \text{Res}\left(\frac{z}{2z^2 + 1}, -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}\right) = \frac{-\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}}{2 \left( -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \right)} = \frac{-\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}}{2 \cdot -\frac{2i}{\sqrt{2}}} = \frac{1}{4} \] Summing these residues gives \( \frac{1}{4} + \frac{1}{4} = \frac{1}{2} \). By the residue theorem, the integral is \( 2\pi i \) times the sum of the residues: \[ \int_C \frac{z}{2z^2 + 1} \, dz = 2\pi i \cdot \frac{1}{2} = \pi i \] Thus, the value of the contour integral is \(\boxed{\pi i}\).

The denominator \( 2z^2 + 1 = 0 \) gives solutions \( z = \pm \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \). Both poles have a magnitude of \( \frac{1}{\sqrt{2}} \), which is less than 1, so they lie inside the unit circle. Using the residue theorem, we calculate the residues at these poles. The integrand can be written as \( \frac{z}{2(z - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}})(z + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}})} \). For a simple pole \( z_0 \), the residue is given by: \[ \text{Res}(f, z_0) = \lim_{z \to z_0} (z - z_0) \cdot \frac{z}{2(z - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}})(z + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}})} \] For \( z = \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \): \[ \text{Res}\left(\frac{z}{2z^2 + 1}, \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}\right) = \frac{\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}}{2 \left( \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \right)} = \frac{\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}}{2 \cdot \frac{2i}{\sqrt{2}}} = \frac{1}{4} \] For \( z = -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \): \[ \text{Res}\left(\frac{z}{2z^2 + 1}, -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}\right) = \frac{-\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}}{2 \left( -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \right)} = \frac{-\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}}{2 \cdot -\frac{2i}{\sqrt{2}}} = \frac{1}{4} \] Summing these residues gives \( \frac{1}{4} + \frac{1}{4} = \frac{1}{2} \). By the residue theorem, the integral is \( 2\pi i \) times the sum of the residues: \[ \int_C \frac{z}{2z^2 + 1} \, dz = 2\pi i \cdot \frac{1}{2} = \pi i \] Thus, the value of the contour integral is \(\boxed{\pi i}\).

Let's factor the denominator: \( 2z^2 + 1 = 2(z^2 + \frac{1}{2}) = 2(z - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}})(z + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}) \). So the integrand becomes \( \frac{z}{2(z - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}})(z + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}})} \). That might help in calculating residues. Alternatively, maybe partial fractions? Let me see. If I want to decompose \( \frac{z}{2z^2 + 1} \) into partial fractions. Let’s set \( \frac{z}{2z^2 + 1} = \frac{A}{z - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}} + \frac{B}{z + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}} \). Multiply both sides by \( 2z^2 + 1 \), which is \( 2(z - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}})(z + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}) \), gives: \( z = A(z + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}) + B(z - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}) \). To find A and B, we can substitute z values that simplify the equation. Let me plug in \( z = \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \): Left side: \( \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \). Right side: \( A( \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} ) + B(0) = A( \frac{2i}{\sqrt{2}} ) = A(i \sqrt{2}) \). Therefore, \( A(i \sqrt{2}) = \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \Rightarrow A = \frac{i/\sqrt{2}}{i \sqrt{2}} = \frac{1}{2} \). Similarly, plug in \( z = -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \): Left side: \( -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \). Right side: \( A(0) + B(-\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} ) = B( -\frac{2i}{\sqrt{2}} ) = B(-i \sqrt{2}) \). Therefore, \( -i \sqrt{2} B = -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \Rightarrow B = \frac{ -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} }{ -i \sqrt{2} } = \frac{1}{2} \). So the partial fractions decomposition is \( \frac{1}{2} \left( \frac{1}{z - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}} + \frac{1}{z + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}} \right) \). Therefore, the integral becomes \( \frac{1}{2} \int_C \left( \frac{1}{z - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}} + \frac{1}{z + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}} \right) dz \). Now, integrating each term separately. The integral of \( \frac{1}{z - a} \) around a contour enclosing a is \( 2\pi i \), by Cauchy's integral formula. Since both \( \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \) and \( -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \) are inside the unit circle, each integral will contribute \( 2\pi i \). Therefore, the total integral is \( \frac{1}{2} (2\pi i + 2\pi i) = \frac{1}{2}(4\pi i) = 2\pi i \). So, is the answer \( 2\pi i \)? Wait, but let me verify this with residue theorem instead of partial fractions. Let's recall that for a function \( f(z) = \frac{z}{2z^2 + 1} \), the residues at the poles \( z_0 \) are given by \( \text{Res}(f, z_0) = \lim_{z \to z_0} (z - z_0) f(z) \). First, at \( z = \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \): \( \text{Res}(f, \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}) = \lim_{z \to \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}} (z - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}) \cdot \frac{z}{2z^2 + 1} \). But since \( 2z^2 + 1 = 2(z - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}})(z + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}) \), we can write: \( \text{Res}(f, \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}) = \lim_{z \to \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}} \frac{z}{2(z + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}})} \). Substituting \( z = \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \): \( \frac{ \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} }{ 2( \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} ) } = \frac{ \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} }{ 2( \frac{2i}{\sqrt{2}} ) } = \frac{ \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} }{ \frac{4i}{\sqrt{2}} } = \frac{1}{4} \). Similarly, at \( z = -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \): \( \text{Res}(f, -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}) = \lim_{z \to -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}} (z + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}) \cdot \frac{z}{2z^2 + 1} \). Which becomes: \( \lim_{z \to -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}} \frac{z}{2(z - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}})} \). Substituting \( z = -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \): \( \frac{ -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} }{ 2( -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} ) } = \frac{ -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} }{ 2( -\frac{2i}{\sqrt{2}} ) } = \frac{ -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} }{ -\frac{4i}{\sqrt{2}} } = \frac{1}{4} \). Therefore, each residue is \( \frac{1}{4} \), and the sum is \( \frac{1}{4} + \frac{1}{4} = \frac{1}{2} \). Then, the integral is \( 2\pi i \times \frac{1}{2} = \pi i \). Wait, but earlier with partial fractions, I got \( 2\pi i \). Which one is correct? There's a discrepancy here. Hmm, that's a problem. Let me check my calculations again. Then, integrating each term over the unit circle. Adding them gives \( 4\pi i \), multiplied by \( 1/2 \) gives \( 2\pi i \). But in the residue approach, each residue is \( 1/4 \), so sum is \( 1/2 \), leading to \( 2\pi i \times 1/2 = \pi i \). Hmm, conflicting answers. So I must have made a mistake in one of the methods. Wait, let me check the residue calculation again. Hmm, maybe that's not helpful. Alternatively, write \( e^{2i\theta} = z^2 \), but z is already used. Wait, the integrand in terms of z is \( \frac{z}{2z^2 +1} \). Let me factor out 2z^2: \( \frac{z}{2z^2(1 + \frac{1}{2z^2})} = \frac{1}{2z} \cdot \frac{1}{1 + \frac{1}{2z^2}} \). Then, using the geometric series expansion for \( \frac{1}{1 + \frac{1}{2z^2}} = \sum_{n=0}^\infty (-1)^n \left( \frac{1}{2z^2} \right)^n \), valid when \( | \frac{1}{2z^2} | < 1 \). On the unit circle, |z| = 1, so \( | \frac{1}{2z^2} | = \frac{1}{2} < 1 \), so convergence is okay. Therefore, the integrand becomes \( \frac{1}{2z} \sum_{n=0}^\infty (-1)^n \frac{1}{2^n z^{2n}} } = \frac{1}{2} \sum_{n=0}^\infty \frac{(-1)^n}{2^n} \cdot z^{-(2n +1)} \). Now, integrating term-by-term over the unit circle C. The integral of \( z^k \) around the unit circle is 0 unless k = -1, in which case it's \( 2\pi i \). So, in the series, we have terms \( z^{-(2n +1)} \). So, when does \( -(2n +1) = -1 \)? That is, when 2n +1 = 1, so n = 0. Therefore, only the term with n=0 contributes. So the integral is \( \frac{1}{2} \cdot \frac{(-1)^0}{2^0} \cdot 2\pi i = \frac{1}{2} \cdot 1 \cdot 2\pi i = \pi i \). So this third method gives \( \pi i \), which matches the residue theorem result. Therefore, the partial fractions approach must be flawed. Wait, but why? Let me check. Earlier, when I did partial fractions, I had: \( \frac{z}{2z^2 +1} = \frac{1}{2} \left( \frac{1}{z - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}} + \frac{1}{z + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}} \right) \). But is this correct? Wait, let me verify the partial fractions decomposition again. Starting from \( z = A(z + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}) + B(z - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}) \). Therefore, equate coefficients: Coefficient of z: \( A + B = 1 \). Coefficient of constant term: \( (A - B) \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} = 0 \). From the constant term equation, \( A - B = 0 \Rightarrow A = B \). Then from the first equation, \( A + A = 1 \Rightarrow 2A = 1 \Rightarrow A = 1/2 \), hence B = 1/2. But this contradicts the residue theorem and the Laurent series method. What's the problem here? Wait a second. Let me check the partial fractions again. Wait, when I factor \( 2z^2 +1 \), I have \( 2(z - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}})(z + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}) \). So when I write the original fraction as \( \frac{z}{2z^2 +1} = \frac{A}{z - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}} + \frac{B}{z + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}} \), but actually, shouldn't the denominator in the partial fractions be \( 2(z - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}})(z + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}) \), so the decomposition would have denominators without the 2? Wait, maybe the error is here. Let me start over. The denominator is \( 2z^2 +1 \). To write partial fractions, we need linear factors. So \( 2z^2 +1 = 2(z - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}})(z + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}) \). Therefore, \( \frac{z}{2z^2 +1} = \frac{z}{2(z - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}})(z + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}})} \). So the partial fractions decomposition would be \( \frac{A}{z - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}} + \frac{B}{z + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}} \). Multiply both sides by \( 2(z - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}})(z + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}) \): Left side: z. Right side: 2A(z + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}) + 2B(z - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}). Therefore, equation: z = 2A(z + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}) + 2B(z - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}). Expand right side: 2A z + 2A \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} + 2B z - 2B \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}. Combine like terms: (2A + 2B) z + (2A - 2B) \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}. Set equal to left side: z + 0. Therefore, equations: Coefficient of z: 2A + 2B = 1. Coefficient of constant term: (2A - 2B) \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} = 0. From the constant term equation: 2A - 2B = 0 ⇒ A = B. From the z coefficient: 2A + 2A = 1 ⇒ 4A = 1 ⇒ A = 1/4, B = 1/4. Therefore, the partial fractions decomposition is \( \frac{1/4}{z - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}} + \frac{1/4}{z + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}} \). Wait, so earlier I missed the factor of 2 in the denominator. The original denominator had a factor of 2, so when decomposing, the equation should be multiplied by the entire denominator, which includes the 2, hence leading to coefficients A and B being 1/4 each instead of 1/2. So my initial partial fractions decomposition was incorrect because I forgot the 2 in the denominator. Therefore, integrating gives \( \frac{1}{4} \times 2\pi i + \frac{1}{4} \times 2\pi i = \frac{\pi i}{2} + \frac{\pi i}{2} = \pi i \), which matches the residue theorem and Laurent series results. So my mistake was forgetting the 2 when setting up the partial fractions. That was the error. So the correct answer is \( \pi i \). Therefore, the integral evaluates to \( \pi i \). But let me confirm once more. Using residues, sum of residues is 1/4 + 1/4 = 1/2, multiplied by \( 2\pi i \) gives \( \pi i \). Laurent series gave the same. And corrected partial fractions also lead to \( \pi i \). So that's consistent. Therefore, the correct value is \( \pi i \). So, in conclusion, the integral \( \int_C \frac{z}{2z^2 +1} \, dz \) equals \( \pi i \). **Final Answer** The value of the contour integral is \boxed{\pi i}. </think> To evaluate the contour integral \( \int_C \frac{z}{2z^2 + 1} \, dz \) where \( C \) is the unit circle centered at the origin, we start by identifying the poles of the integrand. The denominator \( 2z^2 + 1 = 0 \) gives solutions \( z = \pm \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \). Both poles have a magnitude of \( \frac{1}{\sqrt{2}} \), which is less than 1, so they lie inside the unit circle. Using the residue theorem, we calculate the residues at these poles. The integrand can be written as \( \frac{z}{2(z - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}})(z + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}})} \). For a simple pole \( z_0 \), the residue is given by: \[ \text{Res}(f, z_0) = \lim_{z \to z_0} (z - z_0) \cdot \frac{z}{2(z - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}})(z + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}})} \] For \( z = \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \): \[ \text{Res}\left(\frac{z}{2z^2 + 1}, \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}\right) = \frac{\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}}{2 \left( \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} + \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \right)} = \frac{\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}}{2 \cdot \frac{2i}{\sqrt{2}}} = \frac{1}{4} \] For \( z = -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \): \[ \text{Res}\left(\frac{z}{2z^2 + 1}, -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}\right) = \frac{-\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}}{2 \left( -\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} - \frac{i}{\sqrt{2}} \right)} = \frac{-\frac{i}{\sqrt{2}}}{2 \cdot -\frac{2i}{\sqrt{2}}} = \frac{1}{4} \] Summing these residues gives \( \frac{1}{4} + \frac{1}{4} = \frac{1}{2} \). By the residue theorem, the integral is \( 2\pi i \) times the sum of the residues: \[ \int_C \frac{z}{2z^2 + 1} \, dz = 2\pi i \cdot \frac{1}{2} = \pi i \] Thus, the value of the contour integral is \(\boxed{\pi i}\).

Problem: Find the limit of the sequence: \[ \lim_{n \to \infty} \left( \frac{1}{n^k} \sum_{j=1}^n j^k - \frac{n}{k+1} \right) \] where \( k \in \mathbb{N} \setminus \{ 0 \} \). Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: \boxed{0}

Reference Answer: Okay, so I need to find the limit of the sequence given by: \[ \lim_{n \to \infty} \left( \frac{1}{n^k} \sum_{j=1}^n j^k - \frac{n}{k+1} \right) \] where \( k \) is a natural number not equal to zero. Hmm, let's see. I remember that when dealing with sums of powers of integers, there are formulas that express these sums in terms of Bernoulli numbers or something like that. But maybe I can approach this using Riemann sums or some approximation? First, let me recall the formula for the sum of the k-th powers of the first n natural numbers. I think it's something like: \[ \sum_{j=1}^n j^k = \frac{n^{k+1}}{k+1} + \frac{n^k}{2} + \text{lower order terms} \] Is that right? Wait, I remember that Faulhaber's formula gives an expression for such sums, and the leading term is indeed \( \frac{n^{k+1}}{k+1} \). The next term involves a Bernoulli number, maybe \( B_1 \), which is \( -\frac{1}{2} \), so the next term might be \( \frac{k}{2}n^{k} \). Let me check that. Yes, according to Faulhaber's formula, the sum \( \sum_{j=1}^n j^k \) can be expressed as: \[ \frac{n^{k+1}}{k+1} + \frac{1}{2}n^k + \sum_{m=2}^k \frac{B_m}{m!} \cdot \frac{k!}{(k - m +1)!} n^{k - m +1} \] where \( B_m \) are Bernoulli numbers. So the leading term is \( \frac{n^{k+1}}{k+1} \), followed by \( \frac{1}{2}n^k \), and then terms with lower powers of n. So, if I substitute this into the expression given: \[ \frac{1}{n^k} \sum_{j=1}^n j^k - \frac{n}{k+1} \] First, let's compute \( \frac{1}{n^k} \sum_{j=1}^n j^k \). Substituting the Faulhaber's formula expression: \[ \frac{1}{n^k} \left( \frac{n^{k+1}}{k+1} + \frac{1}{2}n^k + \text{lower terms} \right ) - \frac{n}{k+1} \] Simplifying the first term: \[ \frac{n^{k+1}}{(k+1)n^k} = \frac{n}{k+1} \] So when we subtract \( \frac{n}{k+1} \), the leading terms cancel out. Then, the next term is: \[ \frac{1}{2}n^k / n^k = \frac{1}{2} \] The lower-order terms, when divided by \( n^k \), will have factors of \( n^{k - m +1} / n^k = n^{-(m -1)} \). Since \( m \geq 2 \) in those terms, \( m -1 \geq 1 \), so all those terms go to zero as \( n \to \infty \). Therefore, the limit should be \( \frac{1}{2} \). Wait, but let me check if the next term after \( \frac{1}{2}n^k \) is positive or negative. The Bernoulli number \( B_1 \) is -1/2, so maybe the coefficient is positive? Let me check the exact coefficient. Looking back at Faulhaber's formula, the general term for the sum \( \sum_{j=1}^n j^k \) is: \[ \frac{1}{k+1}n^{k+1} + \frac{1}{2}n^k + \frac{k}{12}n^{k-1} - \cdots \] Wait, so the coefficient of \( n^k \) is \( \frac{1}{2} \), right? So when we take \( \frac{1}{n^k} \sum_{j=1}^n j^k \), we get: \[ \frac{1}{k+1}n + \frac{1}{2} + \frac{k}{12}n^{-1} - \cdots \] Subtracting \( \frac{n}{k+1} \), the \( n \) terms cancel, leaving: \[ \frac{1}{2} + \frac{k}{12}n^{-1} + \cdots \] As \( n \to \infty \), all the terms with negative powers of \( n \) vanish, so the limit is \( \frac{1}{2} \). Therefore, the answer should be \( \frac{1}{2} \). Wait, but hold on. Is this correct for all \( k \in \mathbb{N} \setminus \{0\} \)? Let me test with \( k = 1 \). For \( k = 1 \), the sum \( \sum_{j=1}^n j = \frac{n(n+1)}{2} \). Then, \[ \frac{1}{n} \sum_{j=1}^n j - \frac{n}{2} = \frac{1}{n} \cdot \frac{n(n+1)}{2} - \frac{n}{2} = \frac{n+1}{2} - \frac{n}{2} = \frac{1}{2} \] So, indeed, the limit is \( \frac{1}{2} \). So for \( k = 1 \), it works. Let's check for \( k = 2 \). For \( k = 2 \), the sum \( \sum_{j=1}^n j^2 = \frac{n(n+1)(2n+1)}{6} \). Let's compute: \[ \frac{1}{n^2} \sum_{j=1}^n j^2 - \frac{n}{3} \] First, compute \( \sum_{j=1}^n j^2 = \frac{n(n+1)(2n+1)}{6} \). Divide by \( n^2 \): \[ \frac{(n+1)(2n+1)}{6n} = \frac{(n+1)(2n +1)}{6n} = \frac{2n^2 + 3n +1}{6n} = \frac{2n^2}{6n} + \frac{3n}{6n} + \frac{1}{6n} = \frac{n}{3} + \frac{1}{2} + \frac{1}{6n} \] Subtracting \( \frac{n}{3} \): \[ \left( \frac{n}{3} + \frac{1}{2} + \frac{1}{6n} \right ) - \frac{n}{3} = \frac{1}{2} + \frac{1}{6n} \] Taking \( n \to \infty \), the second term goes to zero, so the limit is \( \frac{1}{2} \). So that works too. Wait, so the limit is always \( \frac{1}{2} \) regardless of \( k \)? That seems counterintuitive at first, but the examples check out. Let's try \( k = 3 \). The Euler-Maclaurin formula states that: \[ \sum_{j=1}^n f(j) = \int_{1}^{n} f(x) dx + \frac{f(n) + f(1)}{2} + \text{higher-order terms} \] In our case, \( f(x) = x^k \). But just to be thorough, let's try another example, say \( k = 4 \). First, multiply \( n(n+1) = n^2 + n \). However, in this case, the expression is \( \frac{1}{n^k} \sum_{j=1}^n j^k - \frac{n}{k+1} \). Let's see if we can apply Stolz–Cesàro here. Stolz–Cesàro theorem states that if \( \lim_{n \to \infty} \frac{a_{n+1} - a_n}{b_{n+1} - b_n} \) exists, then \( \lim_{n \to \infty} \frac{a_n}{b_n} \) also exists and is equal to the same limit. The next term is \( kn^{k-1} - \frac{k}{2}n^{k-1} = \frac{k}{2}n^{k-1} \). The subsequent terms will involve lower powers of n. So the leading term in the numerator is \( \frac{k}{2}n^{k-1} \). The denominator \( (n+1)^k - n^k \) can be expanded as: \[ n^k + kn^{k-1} + \cdots - n^k = kn^{k-1} + \cdots \] So the leading term is \( kn^{k-1} \). Therefore, the ratio \( \frac{a_{n+1} - a_n}{b_{n+1} - b_n} \) is approximately \( \frac{\frac{k}{2}n^{k-1}}{kn^{k-1}}} = \frac{1}{2} \). The lower-order terms in numerator and denominator will vanish as \( n \to \infty \), so the limit is \( \frac{1}{2} \). Therefore, by Stolz–Cesàro, the original limit is also \( \frac{1}{2} \). So even for \( k = 4 \), the limit is \( \frac{1}{2} \). Therefore, this seems consistent across different values of \( k \). The key takeaway is that regardless of the power \( k \), the second term in the expansion of the sum \( \sum_{j=1}^n j^k \) is always \( \frac{1}{2}n^k \), leading to the constant term \( \frac{1}{2} \) when divided by \( n^k \), and after subtracting the leading \( \frac{n}{k+1} \), we are left with \( \frac{1}{2} \) as the limit. Alternatively, another approach could be to use the Stolz–Cesàro theorem, which is a tool for evaluating limits of sequences that are in an indeterminate form, typically \( \infty / \infty \) or \( 0/0 \). However, in this case, the expression is \( \frac{1}{n^k} \sum_{j=1}^n j^k - \frac{n}{k+1} \). Let's see if we can apply Stolz–Cesàro here. But the expression can be rewritten as: \[ \frac{\sum_{j=1}^n j^k - \frac{n^{k+1}}{k+1}}{n^k} \] So let's set \( a_n = \sum_{j=1}^n j^k - \frac{n^{k+1}}{k+1} \) and \( b_n = n^k \). Then we want to compute \( \lim_{n \to \infty} \frac{a_n}{b_n} \). Stolz–Cesàro theorem states that if \( \lim_{n \to \infty} \frac{a_{n+1} - a_n}{b_{n+1} - b_n} \) exists, then \( \lim_{n \to \infty} \frac{a_n}{b_n} \) also exists and is equal to the same limit. So let's compute \( a_{n+1} - a_n \): \[ a_{n+1} - a_n = \left( \sum_{j=1}^{n+1} j^k - \frac{(n+1)^{k+1}}{k+1} \right ) - \left( \sum_{j=1}^n j^k - \frac{n^{k+1}}{k+1} \right ) = (n+1)^k - \frac{(n+1)^{k+1} - n^{k+1}}{k+1} \] Similarly, \( b_{n+1} - b_n = (n+1)^k - n^k \). Therefore, the limit becomes: \[ \lim_{n \to \infty} \frac{(n+1)^k - \frac{(n+1)^{k+1} - n^{k+1}}{k+1}}{(n+1)^k - n^k} \] Let me compute the numerator: First, expand \( (n+1)^{k+1} \) using the binomial theorem: \[ (n+1)^{k+1} = n^{k+1} + (k+1)n^k + \frac{(k+1)k}{2}n^{k-1} + \cdots +1 \] Thus, \[ (n+1)^{k+1} - n^{k+1} = (k+1)n^k + \frac{(k+1)k}{2}n^{k-1} + \cdots +1 \] Therefore, the numerator: \[ (n+1)^k - \frac{(n+1)^{k+1} - n^{k+1}}{k+1} = (n+1)^k - \left( n^k + \frac{k}{2}n^{k-1} + \cdots + \frac{1}{k+1} \right ) \] Similarly, expand \( (n+1)^k \): \[ (n+1)^k = n^k + kn^{k-1} + \frac{k(k-1)}{2}n^{k-2} + \cdots +1 \] Subtracting the previous expression: \[ (n^k + kn^{k-1} + \frac{k(k-1)}{2}n^{k-2} + \cdots +1 ) - \left( n^k + \frac{k}{2}n^{k-1} + \cdots + \frac{1}{k+1} \right ) \] The \( n^k \) terms cancel. The next term is \( kn^{k-1} - \frac{k}{2}n^{k-1} = \frac{k}{2}n^{k-1} \). The subsequent terms will involve lower powers of n. So the leading term in the numerator is \( \frac{k}{2}n^{k-1} \). The denominator \( (n+1)^k - n^k \) can be expanded as: \[ n^k + kn^{k-1} + \cdots - n^k = kn^{k-1} + \cdots \] So the leading term is \( kn^{k-1} \). Therefore, the ratio \( \frac{a_{n+1} - a_n}{b_{n+1} - b_n} \) is approximately \( \frac{\frac{k}{2}n^{k-1}}{kn^{k-1}}} = \frac{1}{2} \). The lower-order terms in numerator and denominator will vanish as \( n \to \infty \), so the limit is \( \frac{1}{2} \). Therefore, by Stolz–Cesàro, the original limit is also \( \frac{1}{2} \). So this method also confirms the result. Hence, all approaches lead to the same conclusion that the limit is \( \frac{1}{2} \). However, in this case, the expression is \( \frac{1}{n^k} \sum_{j=1}^n j^k - \frac{n}{k+1} \). Let's see if we can apply Stolz–Cesàro here. Stolz–Cesàro theorem states that if \( \lim_{n \to \infty} \frac{a_{n+1} - a_n}{b_{n+1} - b_n} \) exists, then \( \lim_{n \to \infty} \frac{a_n}{b_n} \) also exists and is equal to the same limit. The next term is \( kn^{k-1} - \frac{k}{2}n^{k-1} = \frac{k}{2}n^{k-1} \). The subsequent terms will involve lower powers of n. So the leading term in the numerator is \( \frac{k}{2}n^{k-1} \). The denominator \( (n+1)^k - n^k \) can be expanded as: \[ n^k + kn^{k-1} + \cdots - n^k = kn^{k-1} + \cdots \] So the leading term is \( kn^{k-1} \). Therefore, the ratio \( \frac{a_{n+1} - a_n}{b_{n+1} - b_n} \) is approximately \( \frac{\frac{k}{2}n^{k-1}}{kn^{k-1}}} = \frac{1}{2} \). The lower-order terms in numerator and denominator will vanish as \( n \to \infty \), so the limit is \( \frac{1}{2} \). Therefore, by Stolz–Cesàro, the original limit is also \( \frac{1}{2} \). Divide by \( n^2 \): \[ \frac{(n+1)(2n+1)}{6n} = \frac{(n+1)(2n +1)}{6n} = \frac{2n^2 + 3n +1}{6n} = \frac{2n^2}{6n} + \frac{3n}{6n} + \frac{1}{6n} = \frac{n}{3} + \frac{1}{2} + \frac{1}{6n} \] Subtracting \( \frac{n}{3} \): \[ \left( \frac{n}{3} + \frac{1}{2} + \frac{1}{6n} \right ) - \frac{n}{3} = \frac{1}{2} + \frac{1}{6n} \] Taking \( n \to \infty \), the second term goes to zero, so the limit is \( \frac{1}{2} \). Divide by \( n^3 \): \[ \frac{n^2(n+1)^2}{4n^3} = \frac{(n+1)^2}{4n} = \frac{n^2 + 2n +1}{4n} = \frac{n}{4} + \frac{2}{4} + \frac{1}{4n} = \frac{n}{4} + \frac{1}{2} + \frac{1}{4n} \] Subtract \( \frac{n}{4} \): \[ \frac{1}{2} + \frac{1}{4n} \] Again, as \( n \to \infty \), the limit is \( \frac{1}{2} \). Hence, regardless of \( k \), the limit is \( \frac{1}{2} \). Alternatively, maybe using integrals. Since sums can be approximated by integrals. Let's try that approach. Consider the sum \( \sum_{j=1}^n j^k \). If we approximate this sum by an integral, we can use the integral of \( x^k \) from 1 to n, but maybe more precise approximation would involve the Euler-Maclaurin formula, which connects sums and integrals and includes correction terms. The Euler-Maclaurin formula states that: \[ \sum_{j=1}^n f(j) = \int_{1}^{n} f(x) dx + \frac{f(n) + f(1)}{2} + \text{higher-order terms} \] In our case, \( f(x) = x^k \). So, \[ \sum_{j=1}^n j^k = \int_{1}^{n} x^k dx + \frac{n^k + 1}{2} + \cdots \] Compute the integral: \[ \int_{1}^{n} x^k dx = \frac{n^{k+1} -1}{k+1} \] Therefore, the sum becomes approximately: \[ \frac{n^{k+1} -1}{k+1} + \frac{n^k +1}{2} + \cdots \] Divide by \( n^k \): \[ \frac{n^{k+1}}{(k+1)n^k} - \frac{1}{(k+1)n^k} + \frac{n^k}{2n^k} + \frac{1}{2n^k} + \cdots = \frac{n}{k+1} - \frac{1}{(k+1)n^k} + \frac{1}{2} + \frac{1}{2n^k} + \cdots \] Subtract \( \frac{n}{k+1} \): \[ \left( \frac{n}{k+1} - \frac{1}{(k+1)n^k} + \frac{1}{2} + \frac{1}{2n^k} + \cdots \right ) - \frac{n}{k+1} = -\frac{1}{(k+1)n^k} + \frac{1}{2} + \frac{1}{2n^k} + \cdots \] Combine the terms: \[ \frac{1}{2} + \left( -\frac{1}{(k+1)n^k} + \frac{1}{2n^k} \right ) + \cdots = \frac{1}{2} + \left( \frac{-1}{(k+1)} + \frac{1}{2} \right ) \frac{1}{n^k} + \cdots \] But as \( n \to \infty \), the terms with \( \frac{1}{n^k} \) go to zero, so the limit is \( \frac{1}{2} \). Therefore, this method also gives \( \frac{1}{2} \). So, regardless of the approach, whether using the known expansion from Faulhaber's formula or the Euler-Maclaurin approximation, the result is the same. The limit is \( \frac{1}{2} \). But just to be thorough, let's try another example, say \( k = 4 \). The sum \( \sum_{j=1}^n j^4 \) is known to be: \[ \sum_{j=1}^n j^4 = \frac{n(n+1)(2n+1)(3n^2 + 3n -1)}{30} \] Let's compute: \[ \frac{1}{n^4} \sum_{j=1}^n j^4 - \frac{n}{5} \] First, expand the sum: \[ \frac{n(n+1)(2n+1)(3n^2 + 3n -1)}{30n^4} - \frac{n}{5} \] Simplify numerator: Let me compute the numerator step by step. First, multiply \( n(n+1) = n^2 + n \). Then multiply by \( (2n+1) \): \[ (n^2 + n)(2n + 1) = 2n^3 + n^2 + 2n^2 + n = 2n^3 + 3n^2 + n \] Multiply by \( (3n^2 + 3n -1) \): This will be tedious, but let's do term by term: First, \( 2n^3 \times (3n^2 + 3n -1) = 6n^5 + 6n^4 - 2n^3 \) Second, \( 3n^2 \times (3n^2 + 3n -1) = 9n^4 + 9n^3 - 3n^2 \) Third, \( n \times (3n^2 + 3n -1) = 3n^3 + 3n^2 - n \) Adding all these together: 6n^5 + 6n^4 - 2n^3 + 9n^4 + 9n^3 - 3n^2 + 3n^3 + 3n^2 - n Combine like terms: 6n^5 + (6n^4 + 9n^4) + (-2n^3 + 9n^3 + 3n^3) + (-3n^2 + 3n^2) + (-n) Which is: 6n^5 + 15n^4 + 10n^3 + 0n^2 - n So the numerator is \( 6n^5 + 15n^4 + 10n^3 - n \), and the denominator is 30n^4. However, in this case, the expression is \( \frac{1}{n^k} \sum_{j=1}^n j^k - \frac{n}{k+1} \). Let's see if we can apply Stolz–Cesàro here. Then we want to compute \( \lim_{n \to \infty} \frac{a_n}{b_n} \). Stolz–Cesàro theorem states that if \( \lim_{n \to \infty} \frac{a_{n+1} - a_n}{b_{n+1} - b_n} \) exists, then \( \lim_{n \to \infty} \frac{a_n}{b_n} \) also exists and is equal to the same limit. Therefore, the limit becomes: \[ \lim_{n \to \infty} \frac{(n+1)^k - \frac{(n+1)^{k+1} - n^{k+1}}{k+1}}{(n+1)^k - n^k} \] Let me compute the numerator: First, expand \( (n+1)^{k+1} \) using the binomial theorem: \[ (n+1)^{k+1} = n^{k+1} + (k+1)n^k + \frac{(k+1)k}{2}n^{k-1} + \cdots +1 \] Thus, \[ (n+1)^{k+1} - n^{k+1} = (k+1)n^k + \frac{(k+1)k}{2}n^{k-1} + \cdots +1 \] Therefore, the numerator: \[ (n+1)^k - \frac{(n+1)^{k+1} - n^{k+1}}{k+1} = (n+1)^k - \left( n^k + \frac{k}{2}n^{k-1} + \cdots + \frac{1}{k+1} \right ) \] Similarly, expand \( (n+1)^k \): \[ (n+1)^k = n^k + kn^{k-1} + \frac{k(k-1)}{2}n^{k-2} + \cdots +1 \] Subtracting the previous expression: \[ (n^k + kn^{k-1} + \frac{k(k-1)}{2}n^{k-2} + \cdots +1 ) - \left( n^k + \frac{k}{2}n^{k-1} + \cdots + \frac{1}{k+1} \right ) \] The \( n^k \) terms cancel. The next term is \( kn^{k-1} - \frac{k}{2}n^{k-1} = \frac{k}{2}n^{k-1} \). The subsequent terms will involve lower powers of n. So the leading term in the numerator is \( \frac{k}{2}n^{k-1} \). The denominator \( (n+1)^k - n^k \) can be expanded as: \[ n^k + kn^{k-1} + \cdots - n^k = kn^{k-1} + \cdots \] So the leading term is \( kn^{k-1} \). Therefore, the ratio \( \frac{a_{n+1} - a_n}{b_{n+1} - b_n} \) is approximately \( \frac{\frac{k}{2}n^{k-1}}{kn^{k-1}}} = \frac{1}{2} \). The lower-order terms in numerator and denominator will vanish as \( n \to \infty \), so the limit is \( \frac{1}{2} \). Therefore, by Stolz–Cesàro, the original limit is also \( \frac{1}{2} \). So this method also confirms the result. Hence, all approaches lead to the same conclusion that the limit is \( \frac{1}{2} \). So, \[ \frac{6n^5 + 15n^4 + 10n^3 - n}{30n^4} = \frac{6n^5}{30n^4} + \frac{15n^4}{30n^4} + \frac{10n^3}{30n^4} - \frac{n}{30n^4} \] Simplify each term: \[ \frac{6n^5}{30n^4} = \frac{6n}{30} = \frac{n}{5} \] \[ \frac{15n^4}{30n^4} = \frac{15}{30} = \frac{1}{2} \] \[ \frac{10n^3}{30n^4} = \frac{10}{30n} = \frac{1}{3n} \] \[ - \frac{n}{30n^4} = -\frac{1}{30n^3} \] So putting it all together: \[ \frac{n}{5} + \frac{1}{2} + \frac{1}{3n} - \frac{1}{30n^3} \] Subtract \( \frac{n}{5} \): \[ \left( \frac{n}{5} + \frac{1}{2} + \frac{1}{3n} - \frac{1}{30n^3} \right ) - \frac{n}{5} = \frac{1}{2} + \frac{1}{3n} - \frac{1}{30n^3} \] As \( n \to \infty \), the terms \( \frac{1}{3n} \) and \( -\frac{1}{30n^3} \) go to zero, so the limit is \( \frac{1}{2} \). So even for \( k = 4 \), the limit is \( \frac{1}{2} \). Therefore, this seems consistent across different values of \( k \). The key takeaway is that regardless of the power \( k \), the second term in the expansion of the sum \( \sum_{j=1}^n j^k \) is always \( \frac{1}{2}n^k \), leading to the constant term \( \frac{1}{2} \) when divided by \( n^k \), and after subtracting the leading \( \frac{n}{k+1} \), we are left with \( \frac{1}{2} \) as the limit. Alternatively, another approach could be to use the Stolz–Cesàro theorem, which is a tool for evaluating limits of sequences that are in an indeterminate form, typically \( \infty / \infty \) or \( 0/0 \). However, in this case, the expression is \( \frac{1}{n^k} \sum_{j=1}^n j^k - \frac{n}{k+1} \). Let's see if we can apply Stolz–Cesàro here. But the expression can be rewritten as: \[ \frac{\sum_{j=1}^n j^k - \frac{n^{k+1}}{k+1}}{n^k} \] So let's set \( a_n = \sum_{j=1}^n j^k - \frac{n^{k+1}}{k+1} \) and \( b_n = n^k \). Then we want to compute \( \lim_{n \to \infty} \frac{a_n}{b_n} \). Stolz–Cesàro theorem states that if \( \lim_{n \to \infty} \frac{a_{n+1} - a_n}{b_{n+1} - b_n} \) exists, then \( \lim_{n \to \infty} \frac{a_n}{b_n} \) also exists and is equal to the same limit. So let's compute \( a_{n+1} - a_n \): \[ a_{n+1} - a_n = \left( \sum_{j=1}^{n+1} j^k - \frac{(n+1)^{k+1}}{k+1} \right ) - \left( \sum_{j=1}^n j^k - \frac{n^{k+1}}{k+1} \right ) = (n+1)^k - \frac{(n+1)^{k+1} - n^{k+1}}{k+1} \] Similarly, \( b_{n+1} - b_n = (n+1)^k - n^k \). Therefore, the limit becomes: \[ \lim_{n \to \infty} \frac{(n+1)^k - \frac{(n+1)^{k+1} - n^{k+1}}{k+1}}{(n+1)^k - n^k} \] Let me compute the numerator: First, expand \( (n+1)^{k+1} \) using the binomial theorem: \[ (n+1)^{k+1} = n^{k+1} + (k+1)n^k + \frac{(k+1)k}{2}n^{k-1} + \cdots +1 \] Thus, \[ (n+1)^{k+1} - n^{k+1} = (k+1)n^k + \frac{(k+1)k}{2}n^{k-1} + \cdots +1 \] Therefore, the numerator: \[ (n+1)^k - \frac{(n+1)^{k+1} - n^{k+1}}{k+1} = (n+1)^k - \left( n^k + \frac{k}{2}n^{k-1} + \cdots + \frac{1}{k+1} \right ) \] Similarly, expand \( (n+1)^k \): \[ (n+1)^k = n^k + kn^{k-1} + \frac{k(k-1)}{2}n^{k-2} + \cdots +1 \] Subtracting the previous expression: \[ (n^k + kn^{k-1} + \frac{k(k-1)}{2}n^{k-2} + \cdots +1 ) - \left( n^k + \frac{k}{2}n^{k-1} + \cdots + \frac{1}{k+1} \right ) \] The \( n^k \) terms cancel. The next term is \( kn^{k-1} - \frac{k}{2}n^{k-1} = \frac{k}{2}n^{k-1} \). The subsequent terms will involve lower powers of n. So the leading term in the numerator is \( \frac{k}{2}n^{k-1} \). The denominator \( (n+1)^k - n^k \) can be expanded as: \[ n^k + kn^{k-1} + \cdots - n^k = kn^{k-1} + \cdots \] So the leading term is \( kn^{k-1} \). Therefore, the ratio \( \frac{a_{n+1} - a_n}{b_{n+1} - b_n} \) is approximately \( \frac{\frac{k}{2}n^{k-1}}{kn^{k-1}}} = \frac{1}{2} \). The lower-order terms in numerator and denominator will vanish as \( n \to \infty \), so the limit is \( \frac{1}{2} \). Therefore, by Stolz–Cesàro, the original limit is also \( \frac{1}{2} \). So this method also confirms the result. Hence, all approaches lead to the same conclusion that the limit is \( \frac{1}{2} \). Therefore, the answer should be \( \boxed{\dfrac{1}{2}} \). **Final Answer** \boxed{\dfrac{1}{2}} </think> To find the limit of the sequence: \[ \lim_{n \to \infty} \left( \frac{1}{n^k} \sum_{j=1}^n j^k - \frac{n}{k+1} \right) \] where \( k \in \mathbb{N} \setminus \{ 0 \} \), we start by considering the sum of the \( k \)-th powers of the first \( n \) natural numbers. Additionally, using the Euler-Maclaurin formula and the Stolz–Cesàro theorem, we consistently find that the limit is \( \frac{1}{2} \). Thus, the limit of the sequence is: \[ \boxed{\dfrac{1}{2}} \]

The Euler-Maclaurin formula states that: \[ \sum_{j=1}^n f(j) = \int_{1}^{n} f(x) dx + \frac{f(n) + f(1)}{2} + \text{higher-order terms} \] In our case, \( f(x) = x^k \). Stolz–Cesàro theorem states that if \( \lim_{n \to \infty} \frac{a_{n+1} - a_n}{b_{n+1} - b_n} \) exists, then \( \lim_{n \to \infty} \frac{a_n}{b_n} \) also exists and is equal to the same limit. The next term is \( kn^{k-1} - \frac{k}{2}n^{k-1} = \frac{k}{2}n^{k-1} \). The subsequent terms will involve lower powers of n. So the leading term in the numerator is \( \frac{k}{2}n^{k-1} \). The denominator \( (n+1)^k - n^k \) can be expanded as: \[ n^k + kn^{k-1} + \cdots - n^k = kn^{k-1} + \cdots \] So the leading term is \( kn^{k-1} \). Therefore, the ratio \( \frac{a_{n+1} - a_n}{b_{n+1} - b_n} \) is approximately \( \frac{\frac{k}{2}n^{k-1}}{kn^{k-1}}} = \frac{1}{2} \). Divide by \( n^2 \): \[ \frac{(n+1)(2n+1)}{6n} = \frac{(n+1)(2n +1)}{6n} = \frac{2n^2 + 3n +1}{6n} = \frac{2n^2}{6n} + \frac{3n}{6n} + \frac{1}{6n} = \frac{n}{3} + \frac{1}{2} + \frac{1}{6n} \] Subtracting \( \frac{n}{3} \): \[ \left( \frac{n}{3} + \frac{1}{2} + \frac{1}{6n} \right ) - \frac{n}{3} = \frac{1}{2} + \frac{1}{6n} \] Taking \( n \to \infty \), the second term goes to zero, so the limit is \( \frac{1}{2} \). Divide by \( n^3 \): \[ \frac{n^2(n+1)^2}{4n^3} = \frac{(n+1)^2}{4n} = \frac{n^2 + 2n +1}{4n} = \frac{n}{4} + \frac{2}{4} + \frac{1}{4n} = \frac{n}{4} + \frac{1}{2} + \frac{1}{4n} \] Subtract \( \frac{n}{4} \): \[ \frac{1}{2} + \frac{1}{4n} \] Again, as \( n \to \infty \), the limit is \( \frac{1}{2} \). Hence, regardless of \( k \), the limit is \( \frac{1}{2} \). Alternatively, maybe using integrals. Since sums can be approximated by integrals. Let's try that approach. Consider the sum \( \sum_{j=1}^n j^k \). If we approximate this sum by an integral, we can use the integral of \( x^k \) from 1 to n, but maybe more precise approximation would involve the Euler-Maclaurin formula, which connects sums and integrals and includes correction terms. The Euler-Maclaurin formula states that: \[ \sum_{j=1}^n f(j) = \int_{1}^{n} f(x) dx + \frac{f(n) + f(1)}{2} + \text{higher-order terms} \] In our case, \( f(x) = x^k \). So, \[ \sum_{j=1}^n j^k = \int_{1}^{n} x^k dx + \frac{n^k + 1}{2} + \cdots \] Compute the integral: \[ \int_{1}^{n} x^k dx = \frac{n^{k+1} -1}{k+1} \] Therefore, the sum becomes approximately: \[ \frac{n^{k+1} -1}{k+1} + \frac{n^k +1}{2} + \cdots \] Divide by \( n^k \): \[ \frac{n^{k+1}}{(k+1)n^k} - \frac{1}{(k+1)n^k} + \frac{n^k}{2n^k} + \frac{1}{2n^k} + \cdots = \frac{n}{k+1} - \frac{1}{(k+1)n^k} + \frac{1}{2} + \frac{1}{2n^k} + \cdots \] Subtract \( \frac{n}{k+1} \): \[ \left( \frac{n}{k+1} - \frac{1}{(k+1)n^k} + \frac{1}{2} + \frac{1}{2n^k} + \cdots \right ) - \frac{n}{k+1} = -\frac{1}{(k+1)n^k} + \frac{1}{2} + \frac{1}{2n^k} + \cdots \] Combine the terms: \[ \frac{1}{2} + \left( -\frac{1}{(k+1)n^k} + \frac{1}{2n^k} \right ) + \cdots = \frac{1}{2} + \left( \frac{-1}{(k+1)} + \frac{1}{2} \right ) \frac{1}{n^k} + \cdots \] But as \( n \to \infty \), the terms with \( \frac{1}{n^k} \) go to zero, so the limit is \( \frac{1}{2} \). Therefore, this method also gives \( \frac{1}{2} \). So, regardless of the approach, whether using the known expansion from Faulhaber's formula or the Euler-Maclaurin approximation, the result is the same. The limit is \( \frac{1}{2} \). But just to be thorough, let's try another example, say \( k = 4 \). The sum \( \sum_{j=1}^n j^4 \) is known to be: \[ \sum_{j=1}^n j^4 = \frac{n(n+1)(2n+1)(3n^2 + 3n -1)}{30} \] Let's compute: \[ \frac{1}{n^4} \sum_{j=1}^n j^4 - \frac{n}{5} \] First, expand the sum: \[ \frac{n(n+1)(2n+1)(3n^2 + 3n -1)}{30n^4} - \frac{n}{5} \] Simplify numerator: Let me compute the numerator step by step. First, multiply \( n(n+1) = n^2 + n \). Then multiply by \( (2n+1) \): \[ (n^2 + n)(2n + 1) = 2n^3 + n^2 + 2n^2 + n = 2n^3 + 3n^2 + n \] Multiply by \( (3n^2 + 3n -1) \): This will be tedious, but let's do term by term: First, \( 2n^3 \times (3n^2 + 3n -1) = 6n^5 + 6n^4 - 2n^3 \) Second, \( 3n^2 \times (3n^2 + 3n -1) = 9n^4 + 9n^3 - 3n^2 \) Third, \( n \times (3n^2 + 3n -1) = 3n^3 + 3n^2 - n \) Adding all these together: 6n^5 + 6n^4 - 2n^3 + 9n^4 + 9n^3 - 3n^2 + 3n^3 + 3n^2 - n Combine like terms: 6n^5 + (6n^4 + 9n^4) + (-2n^3 + 9n^3 + 3n^3) + (-3n^2 + 3n^2) + (-n) Which is: 6n^5 + 15n^4 + 10n^3 + 0n^2 - n So the numerator is \( 6n^5 + 15n^4 + 10n^3 - n \), and the denominator is 30n^4. However, in this case, the expression is \( \frac{1}{n^k} \sum_{j=1}^n j^k - \frac{n}{k+1} \). Let's see if we can apply Stolz–Cesàro here. Then we want to compute \( \lim_{n \to \infty} \frac{a_n}{b_n} \). Stolz–Cesàro theorem states that if \( \lim_{n \to \infty} \frac{a_{n+1} - a_n}{b_{n+1} - b_n} \) exists, then \( \lim_{n \to \infty} \frac{a_n}{b_n} \) also exists and is equal to the same limit. Therefore, the limit becomes: \[ \lim_{n \to \infty} \frac{(n+1)^k - \frac{(n+1)^{k+1} - n^{k+1}}{k+1}}{(n+1)^k - n^k} \] Let me compute the numerator: First, expand \( (n+1)^{k+1} \) using the binomial theorem: \[ (n+1)^{k+1} = n^{k+1} + (k+1)n^k + \frac{(k+1)k}{2}n^{k-1} + \cdots +1 \] Thus, \[ (n+1)^{k+1} - n^{k+1} = (k+1)n^k + \frac{(k+1)k}{2}n^{k-1} + \cdots +1 \] Therefore, the numerator: \[ (n+1)^k - \frac{(n+1)^{k+1} - n^{k+1}}{k+1} = (n+1)^k - \left( n^k + \frac{k}{2}n^{k-1} + \cdots + \frac{1}{k+1} \right ) \] Similarly, expand \( (n+1)^k \): \[ (n+1)^k = n^k + kn^{k-1} + \frac{k(k-1)}{2}n^{k-2} + \cdots +1 \] Subtracting the previous expression: \[ (n^k + kn^{k-1} + \frac{k(k-1)}{2}n^{k-2} + \cdots +1 ) - \left( n^k + \frac{k}{2}n^{k-1} + \cdots + \frac{1}{k+1} \right ) \] The \( n^k \) terms cancel. The next term is \( kn^{k-1} - \frac{k}{2}n^{k-1} = \frac{k}{2}n^{k-1} \). The subsequent terms will involve lower powers of n. So the leading term in the numerator is \( \frac{k}{2}n^{k-1} \). The denominator \( (n+1)^k - n^k \) can be expanded as: \[ n^k + kn^{k-1} + \cdots - n^k = kn^{k-1} + \cdots \] So the leading term is \( kn^{k-1} \). Therefore, the ratio \( \frac{a_{n+1} - a_n}{b_{n+1} - b_n} \) is approximately \( \frac{\frac{k}{2}n^{k-1}}{kn^{k-1}}} = \frac{1}{2} \). The lower-order terms in numerator and denominator will vanish as \( n \to \infty \), so the limit is \( \frac{1}{2} \). Therefore, by Stolz–Cesàro, the original limit is also \( \frac{1}{2} \). So this method also confirms the result. Hence, all approaches lead to the same conclusion that the limit is \( \frac{1}{2} \). So, \[ \frac{6n^5 + 15n^4 + 10n^3 - n}{30n^4} = \frac{6n^5}{30n^4} + \frac{15n^4}{30n^4} + \frac{10n^3}{30n^4} - \frac{n}{30n^4} \] Simplify each term: \[ \frac{6n^5}{30n^4} = \frac{6n}{30} = \frac{n}{5} \] \[ \frac{15n^4}{30n^4} = \frac{15}{30} = \frac{1}{2} \] \[ \frac{10n^3}{30n^4} = \frac{10}{30n} = \frac{1}{3n} \] \[ - \frac{n}{30n^4} = -\frac{1}{30n^3} \] So putting it all together: \[ \frac{n}{5} + \frac{1}{2} + \frac{1}{3n} - \frac{1}{30n^3} \] Subtract \( \frac{n}{5} \): \[ \left( \frac{n}{5} + \frac{1}{2} + \frac{1}{3n} - \frac{1}{30n^3} \right ) - \frac{n}{5} = \frac{1}{2} + \frac{1}{3n} - \frac{1}{30n^3} \] As \( n \to \infty \), the terms \( \frac{1}{3n} \) and \( -\frac{1}{30n^3} \) go to zero, so the limit is \( \frac{1}{2} \). So even for \( k = 4 \), the limit is \( \frac{1}{2} \). Therefore, this seems consistent across different values of \( k \). The key takeaway is that regardless of the power \( k \), the second term in the expansion of the sum \( \sum_{j=1}^n j^k \) is always \( \frac{1}{2}n^k \), leading to the constant term \( \frac{1}{2} \) when divided by \( n^k \), and after subtracting the leading \( \frac{n}{k+1} \), we are left with \( \frac{1}{2} \) as the limit. Alternatively, another approach could be to use the Stolz–Cesàro theorem, which is a tool for evaluating limits of sequences that are in an indeterminate form, typically \( \infty / \infty \) or \( 0/0 \). However, in this case, the expression is \( \frac{1}{n^k} \sum_{j=1}^n j^k - \frac{n}{k+1} \). Let's see if we can apply Stolz–Cesàro here. But the expression can be rewritten as: \[ \frac{\sum_{j=1}^n j^k - \frac{n^{k+1}}{k+1}}{n^k} \] So let's set \( a_n = \sum_{j=1}^n j^k - \frac{n^{k+1}}{k+1} \) and \( b_n = n^k \). Then we want to compute \( \lim_{n \to \infty} \frac{a_n}{b_n} \). Stolz–Cesàro theorem states that if \( \lim_{n \to \infty} \frac{a_{n+1} - a_n}{b_{n+1} - b_n} \) exists, then \( \lim_{n \to \infty} \frac{a_n}{b_n} \) also exists and is equal to the same limit. So let's compute \( a_{n+1} - a_n \): \[ a_{n+1} - a_n = \left( \sum_{j=1}^{n+1} j^k - \frac{(n+1)^{k+1}}{k+1} \right ) - \left( \sum_{j=1}^n j^k - \frac{n^{k+1}}{k+1} \right ) = (n+1)^k - \frac{(n+1)^{k+1} - n^{k+1}}{k+1} \] Similarly, \( b_{n+1} - b_n = (n+1)^k - n^k \). Therefore, the limit becomes: \[ \lim_{n \to \infty} \frac{(n+1)^k - \frac{(n+1)^{k+1} - n^{k+1}}{k+1}}{(n+1)^k - n^k} \] Let me compute the numerator: First, expand \( (n+1)^{k+1} \) using the binomial theorem: \[ (n+1)^{k+1} = n^{k+1} + (k+1)n^k + \frac{(k+1)k}{2}n^{k-1} + \cdots +1 \] Thus, \[ (n+1)^{k+1} - n^{k+1} = (k+1)n^k + \frac{(k+1)k}{2}n^{k-1} + \cdots +1 \] Therefore, the numerator: \[ (n+1)^k - \frac{(n+1)^{k+1} - n^{k+1}}{k+1} = (n+1)^k - \left( n^k + \frac{k}{2}n^{k-1} + \cdots + \frac{1}{k+1} \right ) \] Similarly, expand \( (n+1)^k \): \[ (n+1)^k = n^k + kn^{k-1} + \frac{k(k-1)}{2}n^{k-2} + \cdots +1 \] Subtracting the previous expression: \[ (n^k + kn^{k-1} + \frac{k(k-1)}{2}n^{k-2} + \cdots +1 ) - \left( n^k + \frac{k}{2}n^{k-1} + \cdots + \frac{1}{k+1} \right ) \] The \( n^k \) terms cancel. The next term is \( kn^{k-1} - \frac{k}{2}n^{k-1} = \frac{k}{2}n^{k-1} \). The subsequent terms will involve lower powers of n. So the leading term in the numerator is \( \frac{k}{2}n^{k-1} \). The denominator \( (n+1)^k - n^k \) can be expanded as: \[ n^k + kn^{k-1} + \cdots - n^k = kn^{k-1} + \cdots \] So the leading term is \( kn^{k-1} \). Therefore, the ratio \( \frac{a_{n+1} - a_n}{b_{n+1} - b_n} \) is approximately \( \frac{\frac{k}{2}n^{k-1}}{kn^{k-1}}} = \frac{1}{2} \). The lower-order terms in numerator and denominator will vanish as \( n \to \infty \), so the limit is \( \frac{1}{2} \). Therefore, by Stolz–Cesàro, the original limit is also \( \frac{1}{2} \). So this method also confirms the result. Hence, all approaches lead to the same conclusion that the limit is \( \frac{1}{2} \). Therefore, the answer should be \( \boxed{\dfrac{1}{2}} \). **Final Answer** \boxed{\dfrac{1}{2}} </think> To find the limit of the sequence: \[ \lim_{n \to \infty} \left( \frac{1}{n^k} \sum_{j=1}^n j^k - \frac{n}{k+1} \right) \] where \( k \in \mathbb{N} \setminus \{ 0 \} \), we start by considering the sum of the \( k \)-th powers of the first \( n \) natural numbers. Additionally, using the Euler-Maclaurin formula and the Stolz–Cesàro theorem, we consistently find that the limit is \( \frac{1}{2} \). Thus, the limit of the sequence is: \[ \boxed{\dfrac{1}{2}} \]

The Euler-Maclaurin formula states that: \[ \sum_{j=1}^n f(j) = \int_{1}^{n} f(x) dx + \frac{f(n) + f(1)}{2} + \text{higher-order terms} \] In our case, \( f(x) = x^k \). Stolz–Cesàro theorem states that if \( \lim_{n \to \infty} \frac{a_{n+1} - a_n}{b_{n+1} - b_n} \) exists, then \( \lim_{n \to \infty} \frac{a_n}{b_n} \) also exists and is equal to the same limit. The next term is \( kn^{k-1} - \frac{k}{2}n^{k-1} = \frac{k}{2}n^{k-1} \). The subsequent terms will involve lower powers of n. So the leading term in the numerator is \( \frac{k}{2}n^{k-1} \). The denominator \( (n+1)^k - n^k \) can be expanded as: \[ n^k + kn^{k-1} + \cdots - n^k = kn^{k-1} + \cdots \] So the leading term is \( kn^{k-1} \). Therefore, the ratio \( \frac{a_{n+1} - a_n}{b_{n+1} - b_n} \) is approximately \( \frac{\frac{k}{2}n^{k-1}}{kn^{k-1}}} = \frac{1}{2} \). Divide by \( n^2 \): \[ \frac{(n+1)(2n+1)}{6n} = \frac{(n+1)(2n +1)}{6n} = \frac{2n^2 + 3n +1}{6n} = \frac{2n^2}{6n} + \frac{3n}{6n} + \frac{1}{6n} = \frac{n}{3} + \frac{1}{2} + \frac{1}{6n} \] Subtracting \( \frac{n}{3} \): \[ \left( \frac{n}{3} + \frac{1}{2} + \frac{1}{6n} \right ) - \frac{n}{3} = \frac{1}{2} + \frac{1}{6n} \] Taking \( n \to \infty \), the second term goes to zero, so the limit is \( \frac{1}{2} \). Divide by \( n^3 \): \[ \frac{n^2(n+1)^2}{4n^3} = \frac{(n+1)^2}{4n} = \frac{n^2 + 2n +1}{4n} = \frac{n}{4} + \frac{2}{4} + \frac{1}{4n} = \frac{n}{4} + \frac{1}{2} + \frac{1}{4n} \] Subtract \( \frac{n}{4} \): \[ \frac{1}{2} + \frac{1}{4n} \] Again, as \( n \to \infty \), the limit is \( \frac{1}{2} \).
