Hence, regardless of \( k \), the limit is \( \frac{1}{2} \). Alternatively, maybe using integrals. Since sums can be approximated by integrals. Let's try that approach. Consider the sum \( \sum_{j=1}^n j^k \). If we approximate this sum by an integral, we can use the integral of \( x^k \) from 1 to n, but maybe more precise approximation would involve the Euler-Maclaurin formula, which connects sums and integrals and includes correction terms. The Euler-Maclaurin formula states that: \[ \sum_{j=1}^n f(j) = \int_{1}^{n} f(x) dx + \frac{f(n) + f(1)}{2} + \text{higher-order terms} \] In our case, \( f(x) = x^k \). So, \[ \sum_{j=1}^n j^k = \int_{1}^{n} x^k dx + \frac{n^k + 1}{2} + \cdots \] Compute the integral: \[ \int_{1}^{n} x^k dx = \frac{n^{k+1} -1}{k+1} \] Therefore, the sum becomes approximately: \[ \frac{n^{k+1} -1}{k+1} + \frac{n^k +1}{2} + \cdots \] Divide by \( n^k \): \[ \frac{n^{k+1}}{(k+1)n^k} - \frac{1}{(k+1)n^k} + \frac{n^k}{2n^k} + \frac{1}{2n^k} + \cdots = \frac{n}{k+1} - \frac{1}{(k+1)n^k} + \frac{1}{2} + \frac{1}{2n^k} + \cdots \] Subtract \( \frac{n}{k+1} \): \[ \left( \frac{n}{k+1} - \frac{1}{(k+1)n^k} + \frac{1}{2} + \frac{1}{2n^k} + \cdots \right ) - \frac{n}{k+1} = -\frac{1}{(k+1)n^k} + \frac{1}{2} + \frac{1}{2n^k} + \cdots \] Combine the terms: \[ \frac{1}{2} + \left( -\frac{1}{(k+1)n^k} + \frac{1}{2n^k} \right ) + \cdots = \frac{1}{2} + \left( \frac{-1}{(k+1)} + \frac{1}{2} \right ) \frac{1}{n^k} + \cdots \] But as \( n \to \infty \), the terms with \( \frac{1}{n^k} \) go to zero, so the limit is \( \frac{1}{2} \). Therefore, this method also gives \( \frac{1}{2} \). So, regardless of the approach, whether using the known expansion from Faulhaber's formula or the Euler-Maclaurin approximation, the result is the same. The limit is \( \frac{1}{2} \). But just to be thorough, let's try another example, say \( k = 4 \). The sum \( \sum_{j=1}^n j^4 \) is known to be: \[ \sum_{j=1}^n j^4 = \frac{n(n+1)(2n+1)(3n^2 + 3n -1)}{30} \] Let's compute: \[ \frac{1}{n^4} \sum_{j=1}^n j^4 - \frac{n}{5} \] First, expand the sum: \[ \frac{n(n+1)(2n+1)(3n^2 + 3n -1)}{30n^4} - \frac{n}{5} \] Simplify numerator: Let me compute the numerator step by step. First, multiply \( n(n+1) = n^2 + n \). Then multiply by \( (2n+1) \): \[ (n^2 + n)(2n + 1) = 2n^3 + n^2 + 2n^2 + n = 2n^3 + 3n^2 + n \] Multiply by \( (3n^2 + 3n -1) \): This will be tedious, but let's do term by term: First, \( 2n^3 \times (3n^2 + 3n -1) = 6n^5 + 6n^4 - 2n^3 \) Second, \( 3n^2 \times (3n^2 + 3n -1) = 9n^4 + 9n^3 - 3n^2 \) Third, \( n \times (3n^2 + 3n -1) = 3n^3 + 3n^2 - n \) Adding all these together: 6n^5 + 6n^4 - 2n^3 + 9n^4 + 9n^3 - 3n^2 + 3n^3 + 3n^2 - n Combine like terms: 6n^5 + (6n^4 + 9n^4) + (-2n^3 + 9n^3 + 3n^3) + (-3n^2 + 3n^2) + (-n) Which is: 6n^5 + 15n^4 + 10n^3 + 0n^2 - n So the numerator is \( 6n^5 + 15n^4 + 10n^3 - n \), and the denominator is 30n^4. However, in this case, the expression is \( \frac{1}{n^k} \sum_{j=1}^n j^k - \frac{n}{k+1} \). Let's see if we can apply Stolz–Cesàro here. Then we want to compute \( \lim_{n \to \infty} \frac{a_n}{b_n} \). Stolz–Cesàro theorem states that if \( \lim_{n \to \infty} \frac{a_{n+1} - a_n}{b_{n+1} - b_n} \) exists, then \( \lim_{n \to \infty} \frac{a_n}{b_n} \) also exists and is equal to the same limit. Therefore, the limit becomes: \[ \lim_{n \to \infty} \frac{(n+1)^k - \frac{(n+1)^{k+1} - n^{k+1}}{k+1}}{(n+1)^k - n^k} \] Let me compute the numerator: First, expand \( (n+1)^{k+1} \) using the binomial theorem: \[ (n+1)^{k+1} = n^{k+1} + (k+1)n^k + \frac{(k+1)k}{2}n^{k-1} + \cdots +1 \] Thus, \[ (n+1)^{k+1} - n^{k+1} = (k+1)n^k + \frac{(k+1)k}{2}n^{k-1} + \cdots +1 \] Therefore, the numerator: \[ (n+1)^k - \frac{(n+1)^{k+1} - n^{k+1}}{k+1} = (n+1)^k - \left( n^k + \frac{k}{2}n^{k-1} + \cdots + \frac{1}{k+1} \right ) \] Similarly, expand \( (n+1)^k \): \[ (n+1)^k = n^k + kn^{k-1} + \frac{k(k-1)}{2}n^{k-2} + \cdots +1 \] Subtracting the previous expression: \[ (n^k + kn^{k-1} + \frac{k(k-1)}{2}n^{k-2} + \cdots +1 ) - \left( n^k + \frac{k}{2}n^{k-1} + \cdots + \frac{1}{k+1} \right ) \] The \( n^k \) terms cancel. The next term is \( kn^{k-1} - \frac{k}{2}n^{k-1} = \frac{k}{2}n^{k-1} \). The subsequent terms will involve lower powers of n. So the leading term in the numerator is \( \frac{k}{2}n^{k-1} \). The denominator \( (n+1)^k - n^k \) can be expanded as: \[ n^k + kn^{k-1} + \cdots - n^k = kn^{k-1} + \cdots \] So the leading term is \( kn^{k-1} \). Therefore, the ratio \( \frac{a_{n+1} - a_n}{b_{n+1} - b_n} \) is approximately \( \frac{\frac{k}{2}n^{k-1}}{kn^{k-1}}} = \frac{1}{2} \). The lower-order terms in numerator and denominator will vanish as \( n \to \infty \), so the limit is \( \frac{1}{2} \). Therefore, by Stolz–Cesàro, the original limit is also \( \frac{1}{2} \). So this method also confirms the result. Hence, all approaches lead to the same conclusion that the limit is \( \frac{1}{2} \). So, \[ \frac{6n^5 + 15n^4 + 10n^3 - n}{30n^4} = \frac{6n^5}{30n^4} + \frac{15n^4}{30n^4} + \frac{10n^3}{30n^4} - \frac{n}{30n^4} \] Simplify each term: \[ \frac{6n^5}{30n^4} = \frac{6n}{30} = \frac{n}{5} \] \[ \frac{15n^4}{30n^4} = \frac{15}{30} = \frac{1}{2} \] \[ \frac{10n^3}{30n^4} = \frac{10}{30n} = \frac{1}{3n} \] \[ - \frac{n}{30n^4} = -\frac{1}{30n^3} \] So putting it all together: \[ \frac{n}{5} + \frac{1}{2} + \frac{1}{3n} - \frac{1}{30n^3} \] Subtract \( \frac{n}{5} \): \[ \left( \frac{n}{5} + \frac{1}{2} + \frac{1}{3n} - \frac{1}{30n^3} \right ) - \frac{n}{5} = \frac{1}{2} + \frac{1}{3n} - \frac{1}{30n^3} \] As \( n \to \infty \), the terms \( \frac{1}{3n} \) and \( -\frac{1}{30n^3} \) go to zero, so the limit is \( \frac{1}{2} \). So even for \( k = 4 \), the limit is \( \frac{1}{2} \). Therefore, this seems consistent across different values of \( k \). The key takeaway is that regardless of the power \( k \), the second term in the expansion of the sum \( \sum_{j=1}^n j^k \) is always \( \frac{1}{2}n^k \), leading to the constant term \( \frac{1}{2} \) when divided by \( n^k \), and after subtracting the leading \( \frac{n}{k+1} \), we are left with \( \frac{1}{2} \) as the limit. Alternatively, another approach could be to use the Stolz–Cesàro theorem, which is a tool for evaluating limits of sequences that are in an indeterminate form, typically \( \infty / \infty \) or \( 0/0 \). However, in this case, the expression is \( \frac{1}{n^k} \sum_{j=1}^n j^k - \frac{n}{k+1} \). Let's see if we can apply Stolz–Cesàro here. But the expression can be rewritten as: \[ \frac{\sum_{j=1}^n j^k - \frac{n^{k+1}}{k+1}}{n^k} \] So let's set \( a_n = \sum_{j=1}^n j^k - \frac{n^{k+1}}{k+1} \) and \( b_n = n^k \). Then we want to compute \( \lim_{n \to \infty} \frac{a_n}{b_n} \). Stolz–Cesàro theorem states that if \( \lim_{n \to \infty} \frac{a_{n+1} - a_n}{b_{n+1} - b_n} \) exists, then \( \lim_{n \to \infty} \frac{a_n}{b_n} \) also exists and is equal to the same limit. So let's compute \( a_{n+1} - a_n \): \[ a_{n+1} - a_n = \left( \sum_{j=1}^{n+1} j^k - \frac{(n+1)^{k+1}}{k+1} \right ) - \left( \sum_{j=1}^n j^k - \frac{n^{k+1}}{k+1} \right ) = (n+1)^k - \frac{(n+1)^{k+1} - n^{k+1}}{k+1} \] Similarly, \( b_{n+1} - b_n = (n+1)^k - n^k \). Therefore, the limit becomes: \[ \lim_{n \to \infty} \frac{(n+1)^k - \frac{(n+1)^{k+1} - n^{k+1}}{k+1}}{(n+1)^k - n^k} \] Let me compute the numerator: First, expand \( (n+1)^{k+1} \) using the binomial theorem: \[ (n+1)^{k+1} = n^{k+1} + (k+1)n^k + \frac{(k+1)k}{2}n^{k-1} + \cdots +1 \] Thus, \[ (n+1)^{k+1} - n^{k+1} = (k+1)n^k + \frac{(k+1)k}{2}n^{k-1} + \cdots +1 \] Therefore, the numerator: \[ (n+1)^k - \frac{(n+1)^{k+1} - n^{k+1}}{k+1} = (n+1)^k - \left( n^k + \frac{k}{2}n^{k-1} + \cdots + \frac{1}{k+1} \right ) \] Similarly, expand \( (n+1)^k \): \[ (n+1)^k = n^k + kn^{k-1} + \frac{k(k-1)}{2}n^{k-2} + \cdots +1 \] Subtracting the previous expression: \[ (n^k + kn^{k-1} + \frac{k(k-1)}{2}n^{k-2} + \cdots +1 ) - \left( n^k + \frac{k}{2}n^{k-1} + \cdots + \frac{1}{k+1} \right ) \] The \( n^k \) terms cancel. The next term is \( kn^{k-1} - \frac{k}{2}n^{k-1} = \frac{k}{2}n^{k-1} \). The subsequent terms will involve lower powers of n. So the leading term in the numerator is \( \frac{k}{2}n^{k-1} \). The denominator \( (n+1)^k - n^k \) can be expanded as: \[ n^k + kn^{k-1} + \cdots - n^k = kn^{k-1} + \cdots \] So the leading term is \( kn^{k-1} \). Therefore, the ratio \( \frac{a_{n+1} - a_n}{b_{n+1} - b_n} \) is approximately \( \frac{\frac{k}{2}n^{k-1}}{kn^{k-1}}} = \frac{1}{2} \). The lower-order terms in numerator and denominator will vanish as \( n \to \infty \), so the limit is \( \frac{1}{2} \). Therefore, by Stolz–Cesàro, the original limit is also \( \frac{1}{2} \). So this method also confirms the result. Hence, all approaches lead to the same conclusion that the limit is \( \frac{1}{2} \). Therefore, the answer should be \( \boxed{\dfrac{1}{2}} \). **Final Answer** \boxed{\dfrac{1}{2}} </think> To find the limit of the sequence: \[ \lim_{n \to \infty} \left( \frac{1}{n^k} \sum_{j=1}^n j^k - \frac{n}{k+1} \right) \] where \( k \in \mathbb{N} \setminus \{ 0 \} \), we start by considering the sum of the \( k \)-th powers of the first \( n \) natural numbers. Additionally, using the Euler-Maclaurin formula and the Stolz–Cesàro theorem, we consistently find that the limit is \( \frac{1}{2} \). Thus, the limit of the sequence is: \[ \boxed{\dfrac{1}{2}} \]

Therefore, the answer should be \( \boxed{\dfrac{1}{2}} \). **Final Answer** \boxed{\dfrac{1}{2}} </think> To find the limit of the sequence: \[ \lim_{n \to \infty} \left( \frac{1}{n^k} \sum_{j=1}^n j^k - \frac{n}{k+1} \right) \] where \( k \in \mathbb{N} \setminus \{ 0 \} \), we start by considering the sum of the \( k \)-th powers of the first \( n \) natural numbers. Using Faulhaber's formula, we know that: \[ \sum_{j=1}^n j^k = \frac{n^{k+1}}{k+1} + \frac{1}{2}n^k + \text{lower order terms} \] Dividing this sum by \( n^k \) gives: \[ \frac{1}{n^k} \sum_{j=1}^n j^k = \frac{n}{k+1} + \frac{1}{2} + \text{lower order terms} \] Subtracting \( \frac{n}{k+1} \) from this expression leaves: \[ \frac{1}{2} + \text{lower order terms} \] As \( n \to \infty \), the lower order terms (which involve negative powers of \( n \)) vanish, leaving the limit as \( \frac{1}{2} \). To verify this, we can use specific examples for different values of \( k \): - For \( k = 1 \), the sum \( \sum_{j=1}^n j \) leads to the limit \( \frac{1}{2} \). - For \( k = 2 \), the sum \( \sum_{j=1}^n j^2 \) also results in the limit \( \frac{1}{2} \). - For \( k = 3 \), the sum \( \sum_{j=1}^n j^3 \) similarly confirms the limit \( \frac{1}{2} \). Additionally, using the Euler-Maclaurin formula and the Stolz–Cesàro theorem, we consistently find that the limit is \( \frac{1}{2} \). Thus, the limit of the sequence is: \[ \boxed{\dfrac{1}{2}} \]

Problem: Find the limit of the sequence: \[ \lim_{n \to \infty} \left( \frac{1}{n^k} \sum_{j=1}^n j^k - \frac{n}{k+1} \right) \] where \( k \in \mathbb{N} \setminus \{ 0 \} \). Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: \boxed{0}

Reference Answer: Okay, so I need to find the limit of the sequence given by: \[ \lim_{n \to \infty} \left( \frac{1}{n^k} \sum_{j=1}^n j^k - \frac{n}{k+1} \right) \] where \( k \) is a natural number not equal to zero. Hmm, let's see. I remember that when dealing with sums of powers of integers, there are formulas that express these sums in terms of Bernoulli numbers or something like that. But maybe I can approach this using Riemann sums or some approximation? First, let me recall the formula for the sum of the k-th powers of the first n natural numbers. I think it's something like: \[ \sum_{j=1}^n j^k = \frac{n^{k+1}}{k+1} + \frac{n^k}{2} + \text{lower order terms} \] Is that right? Wait, I remember that Faulhaber's formula gives an expression for such sums, and the leading term is indeed \( \frac{n^{k+1}}{k+1} \). The next term involves a Bernoulli number, maybe \( B_1 \), which is \( -\frac{1}{2} \), so the next term might be \( \frac{k}{2}n^{k} \). Let me check that. Yes, according to Faulhaber's formula, the sum \( \sum_{j=1}^n j^k \) can be expressed as: \[ \frac{n^{k+1}}{k+1} + \frac{1}{2}n^k + \sum_{m=2}^k \frac{B_m}{m!} \cdot \frac{k!}{(k - m +1)!} n^{k - m +1} \] where \( B_m \) are Bernoulli numbers. So the leading term is \( \frac{n^{k+1}}{k+1} \), followed by \( \frac{1}{2}n^k \), and then terms with lower powers of n. So, if I substitute this into the expression given: \[ \frac{1}{n^k} \sum_{j=1}^n j^k - \frac{n}{k+1} \] First, let's compute \( \frac{1}{n^k} \sum_{j=1}^n j^k \). Substituting the Faulhaber's formula expression: \[ \frac{1}{n^k} \left( \frac{n^{k+1}}{k+1} + \frac{1}{2}n^k + \text{lower terms} \right ) - \frac{n}{k+1} \] Simplifying the first term: \[ \frac{n^{k+1}}{(k+1)n^k} = \frac{n}{k+1} \] So when we subtract \( \frac{n}{k+1} \), the leading terms cancel out. Then, the next term is: \[ \frac{1}{2}n^k / n^k = \frac{1}{2} \] The lower-order terms, when divided by \( n^k \), will have factors of \( n^{k - m +1} / n^k = n^{-(m -1)} \). Since \( m \geq 2 \) in those terms, \( m -1 \geq 1 \), so all those terms go to zero as \( n \to \infty \). Therefore, the limit should be \( \frac{1}{2} \). Wait, but let me check if the next term after \( \frac{1}{2}n^k \) is positive or negative. The Bernoulli number \( B_1 \) is -1/2, so maybe the coefficient is positive? Let me check the exact coefficient. Looking back at Faulhaber's formula, the general term for the sum \( \sum_{j=1}^n j^k \) is: \[ \frac{1}{k+1}n^{k+1} + \frac{1}{2}n^k + \frac{k}{12}n^{k-1} - \cdots \] Wait, so the coefficient of \( n^k \) is \( \frac{1}{2} \), right? So when we take \( \frac{1}{n^k} \sum_{j=1}^n j^k \), we get: \[ \frac{1}{k+1}n + \frac{1}{2} + \frac{k}{12}n^{-1} - \cdots \] Subtracting \( \frac{n}{k+1} \), the \( n \) terms cancel, leaving: \[ \frac{1}{2} + \frac{k}{12}n^{-1} + \cdots \] As \( n \to \infty \), all the terms with negative powers of \( n \) vanish, so the limit is \( \frac{1}{2} \). Therefore, the answer should be \( \frac{1}{2} \). Wait, but hold on. Is this correct for all \( k \in \mathbb{N} \setminus \{0\} \)? Let me test with \( k = 1 \). For \( k = 1 \), the sum \( \sum_{j=1}^n j = \frac{n(n+1)}{2} \). Then, \[ \frac{1}{n} \sum_{j=1}^n j - \frac{n}{2} = \frac{1}{n} \cdot \frac{n(n+1)}{2} - \frac{n}{2} = \frac{n+1}{2} - \frac{n}{2} = \frac{1}{2} \] So, indeed, the limit is \( \frac{1}{2} \). So for \( k = 1 \), it works. Let's check for \( k = 2 \). For \( k = 2 \), the sum \( \sum_{j=1}^n j^2 = \frac{n(n+1)(2n+1)}{6} \). Let's compute: \[ \frac{1}{n^2} \sum_{j=1}^n j^2 - \frac{n}{3} \] First, compute \( \sum_{j=1}^n j^2 = \frac{n(n+1)(2n+1)}{6} \). Divide by \( n^2 \): \[ \frac{(n+1)(2n+1)}{6n} = \frac{(n+1)(2n +1)}{6n} = \frac{2n^2 + 3n +1}{6n} = \frac{2n^2}{6n} + \frac{3n}{6n} + \frac{1}{6n} = \frac{n}{3} + \frac{1}{2} + \frac{1}{6n} \] Subtracting \( \frac{n}{3} \): \[ \left( \frac{n}{3} + \frac{1}{2} + \frac{1}{6n} \right ) - \frac{n}{3} = \frac{1}{2} + \frac{1}{6n} \] Taking \( n \to \infty \), the second term goes to zero, so the limit is \( \frac{1}{2} \). So that works too. Wait, so the limit is always \( \frac{1}{2} \) regardless of \( k \)? That seems counterintuitive at first, but the examples check out. Let's try \( k = 3 \). For \( k = 3 \), the sum \( \sum_{j=1}^n j^3 = \left( \frac{n(n+1)}{2} \right )^2 \). Let's compute: \[ \frac{1}{n^3} \sum_{j=1}^n j^3 - \frac{n}{4} \] First, compute \( \sum_{j=1}^n j^3 = \left( \frac{n(n+1)}{2} \right )^2 = \frac{n^2(n+1)^2}{4} \). Divide by \( n^3 \): \[ \frac{n^2(n+1)^2}{4n^3} = \frac{(n+1)^2}{4n} = \frac{n^2 + 2n +1}{4n} = \frac{n}{4} + \frac{2}{4} + \frac{1}{4n} = \frac{n}{4} + \frac{1}{2} + \frac{1}{4n} \] Subtract \( \frac{n}{4} \): \[ \frac{1}{2} + \frac{1}{4n} \] Again, as \( n \to \infty \), the limit is \( \frac{1}{2} \). Hmm, so again \( \frac{1}{2} \). So this seems to hold. Therefore, the answer is \( \frac{1}{2} \). But let me think again why this is happening. The key is that when we expand the sum \( \sum_{j=1}^n j^k \), the leading term is \( \frac{n^{k+1}}{k+1} \), and the next term is \( \frac{1}{2}n^k \). When we divide by \( n^k \), we get \( \frac{n}{k+1} + \frac{1}{2} + \cdots \), and subtracting \( \frac{n}{k+1} \) leaves \( \frac{1}{2} \) plus terms that vanish as \( n \to \infty \). Hence, regardless of \( k \), the limit is \( \frac{1}{2} \). Alternatively, maybe using integrals. Since sums can be approximated by integrals. Let's try that approach. Consider the sum \( \sum_{j=1}^n j^k \). If we approximate this sum by an integral, we can use the integral of \( x^k \) from 1 to n, but maybe more precise approximation would involve the Euler-Maclaurin formula, which connects sums and integrals and includes correction terms. The Euler-Maclaurin formula states that: \[ \sum_{j=1}^n f(j) = \int_{1}^{n} f(x) dx + \frac{f(n) + f(1)}{2} + \text{higher-order terms} \] In our case, \( f(x) = x^k \). So, \[ \sum_{j=1}^n j^k = \int_{1}^{n} x^k dx + \frac{n^k + 1}{2} + \cdots \] Compute the integral: \[ \int_{1}^{n} x^k dx = \frac{n^{k+1} -1}{k+1} \] Therefore, the sum becomes approximately: \[ \frac{n^{k+1} -1}{k+1} + \frac{n^k +1}{2} + \cdots \] Divide by \( n^k \): \[ \frac{n^{k+1}}{(k+1)n^k} - \frac{1}{(k+1)n^k} + \frac{n^k}{2n^k} + \frac{1}{2n^k} + \cdots = \frac{n}{k+1} - \frac{1}{(k+1)n^k} + \frac{1}{2} + \frac{1}{2n^k} + \cdots \] Subtract \( \frac{n}{k+1} \): \[ \left( \frac{n}{k+1} - \frac{1}{(k+1)n^k} + \frac{1}{2} + \frac{1}{2n^k} + \cdots \right ) - \frac{n}{k+1} = -\frac{1}{(k+1)n^k} + \frac{1}{2} + \frac{1}{2n^k} + \cdots \] Combine the terms: \[ \frac{1}{2} + \left( -\frac{1}{(k+1)n^k} + \frac{1}{2n^k} \right ) + \cdots = \frac{1}{2} + \left( \frac{-1}{(k+1)} + \frac{1}{2} \right ) \frac{1}{n^k} + \cdots \] But as \( n \to \infty \), the terms with \( \frac{1}{n^k} \) go to zero, so the limit is \( \frac{1}{2} \). Therefore, this method also gives \( \frac{1}{2} \). So, regardless of the approach, whether using the known expansion from Faulhaber's formula or the Euler-Maclaurin approximation, the result is the same. The limit is \( \frac{1}{2} \). But just to be thorough, let's try another example, say \( k = 4 \). The sum \( \sum_{j=1}^n j^4 \) is known to be: \[ \sum_{j=1}^n j^4 = \frac{n(n+1)(2n+1)(3n^2 + 3n -1)}{30} \] Let's compute: \[ \frac{1}{n^4} \sum_{j=1}^n j^4 - \frac{n}{5} \] First, expand the sum: \[ \frac{n(n+1)(2n+1)(3n^2 + 3n -1)}{30n^4} - \frac{n}{5} \] Simplify numerator: Let me compute the numerator step by step. First, multiply \( n(n+1) = n^2 + n \). Then multiply by \( (2n+1) \): \[ (n^2 + n)(2n + 1) = 2n^3 + n^2 + 2n^2 + n = 2n^3 + 3n^2 + n \] Multiply by \( (3n^2 + 3n -1) \): This will be tedious, but let's do term by term: First, \( 2n^3 \times (3n^2 + 3n -1) = 6n^5 + 6n^4 - 2n^3 \) Second, \( 3n^2 \times (3n^2 + 3n -1) = 9n^4 + 9n^3 - 3n^2 \) Third, \( n \times (3n^2 + 3n -1) = 3n^3 + 3n^2 - n \) Adding all these together: 6n^5 + 6n^4 - 2n^3 + 9n^4 + 9n^3 - 3n^2 + 3n^3 + 3n^2 - n Combine like terms: 6n^5 + (6n^4 + 9n^4) + (-2n^3 + 9n^3 + 3n^3) + (-3n^2 + 3n^2) + (-n) Which is: 6n^5 + 15n^4 + 10n^3 + 0n^2 - n So the numerator is \( 6n^5 + 15n^4 + 10n^3 - n \), and the denominator is 30n^4. So, \[ \frac{6n^5 + 15n^4 + 10n^3 - n}{30n^4} = \frac{6n^5}{30n^4} + \frac{15n^4}{30n^4} + \frac{10n^3}{30n^4} - \frac{n}{30n^4} \] Simplify each term: \[ \frac{6n^5}{30n^4} = \frac{6n}{30} = \frac{n}{5} \] \[ \frac{15n^4}{30n^4} = \frac{15}{30} = \frac{1}{2} \] \[ \frac{10n^3}{30n^4} = \frac{10}{30n} = \frac{1}{3n} \] \[ - \frac{n}{30n^4} = -\frac{1}{30n^3} \] So putting it all together: \[ \frac{n}{5} + \frac{1}{2} + \frac{1}{3n} - \frac{1}{30n^3} \] Subtract \( \frac{n}{5} \): \[ \left( \frac{n}{5} + \frac{1}{2} + \frac{1}{3n} - \frac{1}{30n^3} \right ) - \frac{n}{5} = \frac{1}{2} + \frac{1}{3n} - \frac{1}{30n^3} \] As \( n \to \infty \), the terms \( \frac{1}{3n} \) and \( -\frac{1}{30n^3} \) go to zero, so the limit is \( \frac{1}{2} \). So even for \( k = 4 \), the limit is \( \frac{1}{2} \). Therefore, this seems consistent across different values of \( k \). The key takeaway is that regardless of the power \( k \), the second term in the expansion of the sum \( \sum_{j=1}^n j^k \) is always \( \frac{1}{2}n^k \), leading to the constant term \( \frac{1}{2} \) when divided by \( n^k \), and after subtracting the leading \( \frac{n}{k+1} \), we are left with \( \frac{1}{2} \) as the limit. Alternatively, another approach could be to use the Stolz–Cesàro theorem, which is a tool for evaluating limits of sequences that are in an indeterminate form, typically \( \infty / \infty \) or \( 0/0 \). However, in this case, the expression is \( \frac{1}{n^k} \sum_{j=1}^n j^k - \frac{n}{k+1} \). Let's see if we can apply Stolz–Cesàro here. But the expression can be rewritten as: \[ \frac{\sum_{j=1}^n j^k - \frac{n^{k+1}}{k+1}}{n^k} \] So let's set \( a_n = \sum_{j=1}^n j^k - \frac{n^{k+1}}{k+1} \) and \( b_n = n^k \). Then we want to compute \( \lim_{n \to \infty} \frac{a_n}{b_n} \). Stolz–Cesàro theorem states that if \( \lim_{n \to \infty} \frac{a_{n+1} - a_n}{b_{n+1} - b_n} \) exists, then \( \lim_{n \to \infty} \frac{a_n}{b_n} \) also exists and is equal to the same limit. So let's compute \( a_{n+1} - a_n \): \[ a_{n+1} - a_n = \left( \sum_{j=1}^{n+1} j^k - \frac{(n+1)^{k+1}}{k+1} \right ) - \left( \sum_{j=1}^n j^k - \frac{n^{k+1}}{k+1} \right ) = (n+1)^k - \frac{(n+1)^{k+1} - n^{k+1}}{k+1} \] Similarly, \( b_{n+1} - b_n = (n+1)^k - n^k \). Therefore, the limit becomes: \[ \lim_{n \to \infty} \frac{(n+1)^k - \frac{(n+1)^{k+1} - n^{k+1}}{k+1}}{(n+1)^k - n^k} \] Let me compute the numerator: First, expand \( (n+1)^{k+1} \) using the binomial theorem: \[ (n+1)^{k+1} = n^{k+1} + (k+1)n^k + \frac{(k+1)k}{2}n^{k-1} + \cdots +1 \] Thus, \[ (n+1)^{k+1} - n^{k+1} = (k+1)n^k + \frac{(k+1)k}{2}n^{k-1} + \cdots +1 \] Therefore, the numerator: \[ (n+1)^k - \frac{(n+1)^{k+1} - n^{k+1}}{k+1} = (n+1)^k - \left( n^k + \frac{k}{2}n^{k-1} + \cdots + \frac{1}{k+1} \right ) \] Similarly, expand \( (n+1)^k \): \[ (n+1)^k = n^k + kn^{k-1} + \frac{k(k-1)}{2}n^{k-2} + \cdots +1 \] Subtracting the previous expression: \[ (n^k + kn^{k-1} + \frac{k(k-1)}{2}n^{k-2} + \cdots +1 ) - \left( n^k + \frac{k}{2}n^{k-1} + \cdots + \frac{1}{k+1} \right ) \] The \( n^k \) terms cancel. The next term is \( kn^{k-1} - \frac{k}{2}n^{k-1} = \frac{k}{2}n^{k-1} \). The subsequent terms will involve lower powers of n. So the leading term in the numerator is \( \frac{k}{2}n^{k-1} \). The denominator \( (n+1)^k - n^k \) can be expanded as: \[ n^k + kn^{k-1} + \cdots - n^k = kn^{k-1} + \cdots \] So the leading term is \( kn^{k-1} \). Therefore, the ratio \( \frac{a_{n+1} - a_n}{b_{n+1} - b_n} \) is approximately \( \frac{\frac{k}{2}n^{k-1}}{kn^{k-1}}} = \frac{1}{2} \). The lower-order terms in numerator and denominator will vanish as \( n \to \infty \), so the limit is \( \frac{1}{2} \). Therefore, by Stolz–Cesàro, the original limit is also \( \frac{1}{2} \). So this method also confirms the result. Hence, all approaches lead to the same conclusion that the limit is \( \frac{1}{2} \). Therefore, the answer should be \( \boxed{\dfrac{1}{2}} \). **Final Answer** \boxed{\dfrac{1}{2}} </think> To find the limit of the sequence: \[ \lim_{n \to \infty} \left( \frac{1}{n^k} \sum_{j=1}^n j^k - \frac{n}{k+1} \right) \] where \( k \in \mathbb{N} \setminus \{ 0 \} \), we start by considering the sum of the \( k \)-th powers of the first \( n \) natural numbers. do_single_rollout

Problem: Consider the parallelogram with vertices $(10,45)$, $(10,114)$, $(28,153)$, and $(28,84)$. A line through the origin cuts this figure into two congruent polygons. The slope of the line is $m/n,$ where $m$ and $n$ are relatively prime positive integers. Find $m+n$. Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: \boxed{133}

Reference Answer: Let the first point on the line $x=10$ be $(10,45+a)$ where a is the height above $(10,45)$. Let the second point on the line $x=28$ be $(28, 153-a)$. For two given points, the line will pass the origin if the coordinates are proportional (such that $\frac{y_1}{x_1} = \frac{y_2}{x_2}$). Then, we can write that $\frac{45 + a}{10} = \frac{153 - a}{28}$. Solving for $a$ yields that $1530 - 10a = 1260 + 28a$, so $a=\frac{270}{38}=\frac{135}{19}$. The slope of the line (since it passes through the origin) is $\frac{45 + \frac{135}{19}}{10} = \frac{99}{19}$, and the solution is $m + n = \boxed{118}$.

Format Valid: ✓, Correct: ✗, Reward: 0.00
